Delivery Condition: Court vs. Market vs. Wilderness
Ruling on delivery in market vs wilderness when condition was court.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on delivery in market vs wilderness when the condition specified the judge's court, and explain how custom can modify the ruling.

Core Idea
Custom can redefine what counts as 'court' for kafalah delivery.

In Hanafi kafalah, the guarantor's obligation is to produce the principal for the creditor to bring a claim. The condition may specify a location—here, the judge's court. This passage distinguishes market and wilderness delivery, showing that the ruling turns on whether the place enables the creditor to hold the principal accountable. The attached commentary adds a vital layer: in later times, the market may no longer serve as a court, so the ruling changes with custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and the two delivery scenarios
The condition is to deliver in the judge's court (majlis al-qadi). The guarantor delivers either in the market (suq) or in the wilderness (bariyyah). Determine the ruling for each.
The condition is explicit, but the outcome varies by context.
2
Analyze the rationale for market delivery
The commentary states that in earlier times, judges held court in the market. Thus delivering in the market effectively fulfilled the condition because the creditor could bring the claim there. The guarantor is freed.
This is based on 'urf (custom).
3
Analyze the rationale for wilderness delivery
The wilderness is not a place of judgment; the creditor cannot enforce the claim there. Delivering in the wilderness does not fulfill the condition, so the guarantor remains liable.
Wilderness is akin to absconding or conspiracy to help the principal escape.
4
Apply the principle of changing custom
The commentary notes that in the commentator's era, markets no longer served as courts. Therefore, delivering in the market would not free the guarantor. This shows that the ruling is not static but adapts to changing customs.
The operative principle: the place of delivery must be one where the creditor can hold the principal accountable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1424] (۷) واذا تكفل به على ان يسلمه في مجلس القاضي فسلمه في السوق برئ وان سلمه فى برية لم يبرأ اگر کفیل بنا اس بات کا کہ مکفول بہ کو قاضی کی مجلس میں سپر د کرے گا اور سپر د کیا اس کو بازار میں تو کفیل بری ہو جائے گا اور اگر سپرد کیا جنگل میں تو بری نہیں ہوگا۔ اگر مجلس قضاء میں سپرد کرنے کی شرط تھی لیکن اس نے اس کے بجائے بازار میں مکفول بہ کو سپرد کیا تو بری ہو جائے گا۔ اور جنگل میں سپرد کیا تو بری نہیں ہوگا۔ اس زمانے میں قاضی بازار میں بھی مجرم سے محاسبہ کر لیا کرتے تھے اس لئے بازار میں سپرد کرنے سے بھی مکفول بہ محاسبہ کے قابل ہو گیا۔ اس لئے کفیل بری ہو جائے گا۔ اور جنگل میں قاضی محاسبہ نہیں کر سکتا بلکہ اس قسم سے بھگانے کی سازش ہے۔ اس لئے جنگل میں سپر د کر نے سے کفیل بری نہیں ہو گا۔ اموں ایسے مقام پر سپرد کرنا ضروری ہے جہاں مکفول بہ سے محاسبہ کیا جا سکے۔ اس دور میں بازار اس دور میں بازار میں محاسبہ کرنا ممکن نہیں اس لئے اب بازار میں سپرد کرنے سے کفیل کفالہ سے بری نہیں ہوگا۔
And if he becomes guarantor for him on condition that he will deliver him in the judge's court, then he delivers him in the market, he is freed; but if he delivers him in a desert, he is not freed. If the condition was to deliver in the court, but instead he delivered in the market, he is freed. And if he delivered in the wilderness, he is not freed. In that time, judges used to hold court in the market as well, so delivering in the market also made the principal subject to accounting. Therefore the guarantor is freed. In the wilderness, the judge cannot hold court; rather it is a conspiracy to help him escape. Therefore delivering in the wilderness does not free the guarantor. In our time, it is necessary to deliver in a place where accounting can take place. Since in this era, accounting in the market is not possible, delivering in the market does not free the guarantor.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage addresses a conditional kafalah where the guarantor agreed to deliver the principal specifically in the judge's court. The ruling states that if he delivers in the market instead, he is still freed if the market was a place where the judge used to hold court (as was custom in earlier times). However, if he delivers in a wilderness where no court exists, he is not freed. The Urdu commentary adds an important qualification: the ruling depends on the custom of the time. In the commentator's own time, the market no longer served as a court, so delivering in the market would not free the guarantor. This illustrates how 'urf (custom) can affect the application of a ruling. The core principle is that the place of delivery must enable the creditor to bring the principal before a judge for his claim.

Key Terms
مجلس القاضي (majlis al-qadi): The judge's court or place where he holds sessions to adjudicate cases.
برية (bariyyah): Wilderness or desert; a place where no legal authority is present.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The guarantor is freed because the wilderness is public.

C) The guarantor is not freed because no judge can hold court there.
D) The ruling depends on the season.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi kafalah, the place of delivery must enable the creditor to bring the principal before a judge; custom determines which places suffice.

Death of Principal Terminates Guarantee
Ruling and reasoning for termination of kafalah upon principal's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the guarantor is freed upon the death of the principal and explain the underlying reasoning of impossibility and dependency.

Core Idea
Death of the principal terminates the guarantee — the dependent obligation cannot outlast the original.

You have learned that kafalah is a dependent contract where the guarantor's obligation is subsidiary to the principal's. This lesson examines a natural boundary: what happens when the principal dies? Using the principle that the subsidiary cannot exceed the primary, the Hanafi school concludes that the guarantor is fully discharged, regardless of any property left by the deceased.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is kafalah bil nafs (guarantee of the person). The ruling is that the guarantor (kafeel) is freed (yabra'u) upon the death of the principal (makful bih).
2
Understand the reasoning
The Urdu commentary provides three reasons: (1) impossibility — the guarantor cannot produce a dead person; (2) dependency — when the obligation falls from the principal (asl), it also falls from the dependent (far'); (3) the principal's estate is not liable for this performance guarantee.
This reasoning uses the maxim that the subsidiary cannot be more burdensome than the original.
3
Note the evidence
The author cites an athar (report) from al-Hakam and Hammad, early Kufan scholars. One said the guarantor must pay dirhams; the other said nothing is due. The Hanafi relied-upon view adopts the latter.
4
Recognize the ikhtilaf and tarjih
Although one early scholar held for liability, the established Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah) is that the guarantor is free from both the person and any financial claim arising from the guarantee.
The tarjih is based on the strength of the dependency argument and the impossibility of performance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1425] (۸) واذا مات المكفول به برئ الكفيل اگر مکفول به مرجائے تو کفیل بالنفس کفالہ سے بری ہو جائے گا۔ کفیل نے مجرم کو حاضر کرنے کی کفالت لی تھی لیکن خود مجرم کا انتقال ہو گیا تو کفیل سے کفالہ ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ (۱) مرجانے کی وجہ سے اب حاضر کس کو کرے گا؟ اور مکفول بہ کا مال تو اس کام کے لئے نہیں ہے اس لئے کفالہ ساقط ہو جائے گا (۲) مرنے کی وجہ سے اصیل یعنی مکفول بہ پر حاضر ہونا ساقط ہو گیا اس لئے تابع یعنی کفیل پر بھی ساقط ہو جائے گا (۳) اثر میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن الحكم و حماد انهما قالا في رجل تكفل بنفس رجل فمات الرجل قال احدهما يضمن الدراهم وقال الآخر ليس عليه شيء (الف) (سن سبیھقی ، باب ماجاء فی الكفالة بدن من علی حق ، ج سادس ص ۱۲۸، نمبر ۱۱۴۱۹) اس میں ہے کہ عليه حاشیہ : (پچھلے صفحہ سے آگے ) کہا عبداللہ کے پاس جاؤ کھانے اور بستر کے لئے ۔ عبد اللہ قاضی شریح کے دوسرے بیٹے کا نام ہے (الف) حضرت حکم اور حماد نے فرمایا کوئی آدمی کسی آدمی کا کفیل بالنفس بنا پھر وہ آدمی مر گیا۔ ان میں سے ایک نے کہا درہم کا ضامن ہوگا اور دوسرے نے فرمایا کھیل پر کچھ نہیں ہے
And if the principal dies, the guarantor is freed from the guarantee. If the principal dies, the guarantor of the person is freed from the guarantee. The guarantor had taken the guarantee to produce the criminal, but the criminal himself passed away, so the guarantee falls from the guarantor. (1) Because of death, whom will he produce? And the property of the principal is not for this purpose, so the guarantee falls. (2) Because of death, the obligation of appearance falls from the original (the principal), so it also falls from the dependent (the guarantor). (3) There is evidence for this in athar: From al-Hakam and Hammad that they said regarding a man who became guarantor for the person of a man, then the man died; one of them said he guarantees the dirhams, and the other said there is nothing upon him.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that if the principal (makful bih) dies, the guarantor (kafeel) is discharged from his obligation of kafalah bil nafs. The Urdu commentary elaborates the reasoning: (1) it is impossible to produce a dead person, and the principal's estate is not liable for this guarantee; (2) since the obligation to appear falls from the principal (the asl), it also falls from the guarantor (the far'); (3) a report (athar) from al-Hakam and Hammad mentions a difference of opinion: one said the guarantor becomes liable for the debt (dirhams), the other said nothing is due. The commentary seems to side with the latter, that the guarantor is free. The report shows early ikhtilaf, but the relied-upon Hanafi position is that death releases the guarantor from both the person and any financial liability unless he specifically guaranteed the debt.

Key Terms
أصيل (asil): The principal (the original debtor or the person for whom guarantee is given).
تابع (tabi'): The follower or dependent, i.e., the guarantor whose obligation is derived from the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the guarantor (kafeel) if the principal (makful bih) dies?
A) The guarantor becomes liable for the debt.
B) The guarantor is released from the guarantee.
C) The guarantor must produce the body.
D) The guarantee transfers to the principal's heirs.

Key Takeaway
The death of the principal terminates the guarantee of person, releasing the guarantor from all obligations arising from that guarantee.

Conditional Guarantee: Debt Liability Upon Non-Appearance
Ruling on combined kafalah with condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional guarantee combining person and money, and apply the hadith evidence to similar cases.

Core Idea
A conditional guarantee is valid: failure to produce triggers debt liability but does not cancel the duty to produce.

This lesson examines a composite kafalah where the guarantor promises to produce the principal by a deadline, failing which he becomes liable for the principal's debt. The text rules that both obligations—personal and financial—stand. We explore the proof from the Prophet's practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a conditional guarantee (kafalah bi al-shart). The ruling: upon failure, debt becomes wajib on the guarantor, but personal guarantee remains.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith from Jabir where the Prophet (peace be upon him) took responsibility for debts of deceased believers is cited as evidence.
Hadith reference: Nasa'i and Bukhari.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's conditional promise (if a debt is left, he will pay) shows that a guarantee can be conditioned on a future event.
This establishes the permissibility of conditional guarantees.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Some may argue the condition is invalid or that paying the debt releases the guarantor. The Hanafi view upholds both obligations.
The practical difference: the guarantor must still attempt to produce the principal.

And if he becomes guarantor for his person on the condition that if he does not produce him by such a time, then he is liable for what is upon him (the debt), and it is one thousand; then he did not produce him at that time, so the guarantee of the money becomes binding upon him, and he is not freed from the guarantee of the person. If the guarantor became a guarantor of the person in this manner: 'If I do not produce him at such a time, then I am liable for the debt that is upon him, and it was one thousand;' then he did not produce him at that time, so the guarantee of the money becomes necessary for the guarantor, and he is not freed from the guarantee of the person. In this expression, there are two types of guarantee together: guarantee of money and guarantee of person. And it is with a condition: if he cannot produce him at the time, then the amount of debt he has, I am responsible for it. From 'did not produce' comes the guarantee of person, and from 'I am responsible for the debt' comes the guarantee of money. Now when he failed to produce on time, the debt becomes incumbent on the guarantor. And the guarantee of person (to produce him) is not canceled, because the original obligation is to produce him. The proof for conditional guarantee is this hadith: From Jabir who said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) would not pray over a man who had a debt... He said: 'I am more worthy of every believer than his own self. Whoever leaves a debt, it is upon me; and whoever leaves wealth, it is for his heirs.' In this hadith, the Prophet took responsibility on the condition that if the deceased left a debt, he would be responsible. Thus it is known that conditional guarantee is permissible.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage deals with a composite guarantee: the guarantor (kafeel) makes a conditional promise that if he fails to produce the principal by a specified time, he will be liable for the principal's debt. The ruling states that upon failure to produce, the guarantor becomes liable for the debt (kafalah bil mal) but is not discharged from his original obligation to produce the person (kafalah bil nafs). The Urdu commentary explains that this combines two types of guarantee: the failure triggers a financial guarantee, but the personal guarantee remains because the primary duty (presenting the principal) is not extinguished by the monetary liability. The proof is taken from the hadith of Jabir where the Prophet (peace be upon him) promised to pay the debts of believers who died indebted, which shows the permissibility of conditional guarantees (kafalah bi al-shart). This hadith is cited as evidence that a conditional guarantee is valid and enforceable. The lesson highlights that a guarantor can be bound both to produce a person and to pay a debt in the alternative, but the personal obligation subsists even after the monetary liability is triggered.

Key Terms
كفالة بالمال (kafalah bil mal): Guarantee of debt or financial obligation.
كفالة بالشرط (kafalah bi al-shart): Conditional guarantee where liability depends on a future event (e.g., failure to produce).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A conditional guarantee is valid and creates two distinct obligations: financial liability upon failure, and continuing duty to produce the person.

Kafalah in Hudud and Qisas: Imam Abu Hanifa's View
Dispute on kafalah in hudud and qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling on kafalah bil nafs in hudud and qisas, state the Sahibayn's exception for qadhf, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Hudud are rights of Allah — the court cannot compel a personal guarantee to enforce them.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a defendant can be compelled to provide a personal guarantor (kafalah bil nafs) in hudud and qisas cases. Imam Abu Hanifa forbids compulsion, while the Sahibayn allow it for qadhf due to its personal right component. We will explore the evidence and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling on kafalah bil nafs in hudud and qisas: not permissible, and the court cannot compel it. Note the exception that voluntary kafalah is allowed.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differ: they allow compulsion in the hadd of qadhf because it involves a personal right of the victim.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Sahibayn argue that qadhf has a personal element (the victim's honor), analogous to a debt claim where kafalah is allowed.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa relies on the hadith 'La kafalah fi hadd' (no guarantee in hadd), which is general. The personal right in qadhf does not override the prohibition because the punishment remains a hadd, and hudud are to be avoided by doubts.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: in qadhf, according to Sahibayn, the court can compel a guarantor; according to Abu Hanifa, it cannot. Voluntary kafalah is acceptable in both views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1427] (۱۰) ولا تجوز الكفالة بالنفس فى الحدود والقصاص عند ابي کفالہ بالنفس جائز نہیں ہے حدود اور قصاص میں امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک۔ حدود اور قصاص میں کفالہ بالنفس دینے پر مجبور نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ البتہ وہ دے دے تو کوئی حرج کی بات نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے کہ حدود اور قصاص میں کوشش کی جاتی ہے کہ مجرم پر یہ جاری نہ ہوں۔ اور کفالہ دینے کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ چھوٹتا بھی ہو تو اور مضبوط کیا جائے ۔ اس لئے حد دو اور قصاص میں کفالہ بالنفس دینے پر مجبور نہ کیا جائے (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ حدثني عمر بن شعیب عن ابيه عن جده ان النبي الا الله قال لا كفالة في حد (ب) ( سنن بیھقی ، باب ماجاء فی الكفالة ببدن من علیہ حق ، ج سادس، ص ۱۲۷، نمبر ۱۱۴۱۷) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ حد میں کفالہ بالنفس نہیں ہے۔ نانه صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ حد قذف میں چونکہ بندے کے حقوق ہوتے ہیں اس لئے اس میں کفالہ بالنفس دینے پر مجبور کیا جائے گا۔
And kafalah bil nafs is not permissible in hudud and qisas according to Abu Hanifa. Kafalah bil nafs is not permissible in hudud and qisas according to Imam Abu Hanifa. One will not be compelled to give kafalah bil nafs in hudud and qisas. However, if he gives it, there is no harm. Because in hudud and qisas, efforts are made that they not be carried out on the criminal. And the meaning of giving kafalah is that if he escapes, it is made stronger. Therefore, in hudud and qisas, one should not be compelled to give kafalah bil nafs. (2) There is a hadith: 'Umar ibn Shu'ayb narrated from his father from his grandfather that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: There is no kafalah in hadd.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi). In this hadith, it is said that there is no kafalah bil nafs in hadd. And the Sahibayn (Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) say that in the hadd of qadhf (false accusation of adultery), since there are personal rights (of the accused), one will be compelled to give kafalah bil nafs.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute (ikhtilaf) within the Hanafi school regarding kafalah bil nafs in cases of hudud (fixed punishments for crimes like theft, fornication) and qisas (retaliation for murder or injury). Imam Abu Hanifa's position is that such kafalah is not permissible and the court cannot compel the guarantor to produce the accused in these cases. He bases this on a hadith: 'La kafalah fi hadd' (there is no guarantee in hadd). The reasoning is that the Islamic legal system encourages the avoidance of hudud through doubt and forgiveness; kafalah would facilitate enforcement. However, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differentiate: they hold that in the hadd of qadhf (slander), which involves a personal right of the victim (the right to have his honor vindicated), kafalah is allowed because the victim has a claim similar to a debt. Thus, the court can compel a guarantor in qadhf cases. The commentary notes that Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position in the school. The text also mentions that if someone voluntarily gives kafalah, it is not prohibited (la haraj).

Key Terms
حد (hadd): Fixed punishment prescribed by Allah for specific crimes (e.g., theft, fornication, slander).
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation for murder or injury; considered as a personal right but also involves a divine command.
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of adultery or fornication; a hadd crime with a personal right component.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is permissible and the court can compel a guarantor.
B) It is not permissible, but if given voluntarily, it is acceptable.
C) It is absolutely prohibited even if voluntary.
D) It is permissible only if the victim demands it.

Key Takeaway
In hudud and qisas, the court cannot compel a personal guarantee, but if given voluntarily, it is acceptable — Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position.

The Validity of Conditional Suretyship Combining Person and Debt
Ruling on conditional kafalah bil nafs with debt guarantee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional kafalah bil nafs with a debt guarantee and explain the proof from the hadith.

Core Idea
Conditional suretyship combining personal guarantee and debt guarantee is valid and both obligations remain separate.

This passage addresses a specific form of suretyship where the surety promises to produce the principal and also guarantees the principal's debt if he fails to do so by a deadline. The ruling clarifies that failure triggers the debt guarantee but does not release the surety from the personal guarantee. The proof is drawn from the Prophet's practice of assuming responsibility for the debts of believers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the contractual arrangement described? The surety becomes kafil bil nafs on condition that if he does not produce the principal by a specified time, he becomes daman for the principal's debt. The ruling: if the condition is triggered, the surety must pay the debt but remains obligated to produce the person.
The ruling is stated clearly in the Arabic matn: 'لزمه ضمان المال ولم يبرأ من الكفالة بالنفس'.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites a hadith from Jabir (Nasa'i and Bukhari) where the Prophet (PBUH) said: 'I am more entitled to every believer than his own self. Whoever leaves a debt, it is upon me; and whoever leaves wealth, it is for his heirs.'
This shows the Prophet took conditional responsibility for debts of the deceased, establishing precedent for conditional suretyship.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The proof lies in the Prophet's conditional assumption of liability: he only takes responsibility for the debt if the deceased leaves debt. This matches the conditional nature of the suretyship in the ruling.
The condition 'if he leaves debt' (من ترك دينا فعلي) parallels the condition in the kafalah: 'if I do not present him, I am responsible for his debt'.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The text does not mention any disagreement. The ruling is presented as the established Hanafi position, supported by a clear hadith.
The analytical_toolbox indicates cross_madhhab_status = muttafaq_alayh, meaning no significant dispute.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Since there is no stated ikhtilaf, the ruling is taken as mufta bihi. The proof is considered strong enough to validate the conditional contract.
The hadith is ahad but its meaning (dalala) is clear and gives a qati ruling according to the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالنفس من الكفالة [۱۴۲۶(۹) وان تكفل بنفسه على انه ان لم يواف به في وقت كذا فهو ضامن لما عليه وهو الف فلم يحضره في ذلك الوقت لزمه ضمان المال ولم يبرأ من الكفالة بالنفس
اصل یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ اصل سے ساقط ہو جائے تو کفیل جو فرع ہے اس سے بھی ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ ۱۳۹ (۹) اگر کفیل بالنفس بنا اس طرح کہ اگر میں نے اس کو فلاں وقت میں حاضر نہ کیا تو میں ضامن ہوں اس مال کا جو اس پر ہے اور وہ ایک ہزار تھا، پھر اس وقت میں حاضر نہ کیا تو کفیل پر مال کا ضمان لازم ہو جائے گا اور کفالہ بانفس سے بری نہیں ہوگا۔
اس عبارت میں اسی ساتھ دو قسم کا کفالہ ہے۔ کفالہ بالمال بھی اور کفالہ بانفس بھی ہے۔ اور وہ بھی شرط کے ساتھ ہے کہ فلاں وقت میں فلاں کو حاضر نہ کر سکا تو جتنا اس پر قرض ہے میں اس کا ذمہ دار ہوں تو حاضر نہ کر سکا سے کفالہ با نفس ہوا اور جتنا اس پر قرض ہے اس کا میں ذمہ دار ہوں سے کفالہ بالمال ہو گیا۔ اب وقت پر حاضر نہ کر سکا تو کفیل پر قرض لازم ہو جائے گا۔ اور کفالہ بانفس جو حاضر کرنا تھاوہ بھی ساقط نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اصل تو اس کو حاضر کرنا ہی ہے۔ شرط کے ساتھ کفالہ کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن جابر قال كان النبي الله لا يصلى على رجل عليه دين ... قال انا اولى بكل مؤمن من نفسه من ترک دینا فعلی و من ترک مالا فلورثته (الف) (نسائی شریف باب الصلوة علی من علیه دین ص ۲۰۷ نمبر ۱۹۶۴ار بخاری شریف، باب الدین، کتاب الکفالۃ ص ۳۰۵ نمبر ۲۲۹۸) اس حدیث میں اس شرط پر آپ نے ذمہ داری لی کہ اگر قرض چھوڑا تو میں ذمہ دار ہوں تو معلوم ہوا کہ شرطیہ کفیل بنا جائز ہے۔
From suretyship of the person: (9) And if he becomes surety for the person on condition that if he does not bring him at such a time, then he is guarantor for what is upon him, and it is one thousand, and he did not bring him at that time, then the guarantee of the debt becomes incumbent upon him, and he is not released from the suretyship of the person.
The principle of this issue is that if the principal is discharged, then the surety, being a branch, will also be discharged. (9) If a surety for the person makes a condition that if I do not present him at such a time, then I am guarantor for the debt that is upon him, and it was one thousand, then if he did not present him at that time, the guarantee of the debt becomes incumbent upon the surety, and he will not be released from the suretyship of the person.
In this expression, there are two types of suretyship together: suretyship for the debt and suretyship for the person. And it is with a condition that if he could not present so-and-so at such a time, then I am responsible for whatever debt is upon him. So from 'could not present' comes suretyship for the person, and from 'I am responsible for whatever debt is upon him' comes suretyship for the debt. Now, when he could not present at the time, the debt becomes incumbent upon the surety. And the suretyship for the person, which was to present him, is not waived. Because the original is to present him. The proof for conditional suretyship is this hadith: from Jabir that the Prophet (PBUH) would not pray over a man who had a debt... He said: I am more entitled to every believer than his own self. Whoever leaves a debt, it is upon me; and whoever leaves wealth, it is for his heirs. (Nasa'i, Bab al-Salat 'ala man 'alayhi dayn, p. 207, no. 1964; Bukhari, Bab al-Dayn, Kitab al-Kafalah, p. 305, no. 2298). In this hadith, he took responsibility on this condition: if he leaves a debt, then I am responsible. So it is known that conditional suretyship is valid.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a specific form of combined suretyship. The text first states the ruling in Arabic: if someone becomes a surety for a person (kafalah bil nafs) with the condition that failure to produce the person by a deadline makes the surety liable for the principal's debt, and the surety fails, then he must pay the debt but remains obligated under the personal suretyship—he is not freed from the duty to produce the person. The Urdu commentary explains the principle: if the original debtor is discharged, the surety also is discharged; but here the original obligation to present the person remains, so both guarantees stand. The key point is that the surety has assumed two separate liabilities: one to produce the person, and one to pay the debt if he fails. Failure under the condition activates the debt guarantee but does not cancel the personal guarantee. The proof is a hadith from Jabir where the Prophet (PBUH) refused to pray over a deceased debtor, then later said he is responsible for the debts of believers who leave debt. This shows that conditional responsibility is valid in suretyship.

Key Terms
كفالة بالنفس (kafalah bi al-nafs): Suretyship of the person: an obligation to produce the person guaranteed.
ضمان المال (daman al-mal): Guarantee of debt: the surety becomes liable for the pecuniary obligation of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) The surety is only required to produce the person and not pay the debt.

Key Takeaway
A conditional suretyship combining personal guarantee and debt guarantee is valid; the surety's failure triggers the debt payment but does not cancel the duty to produce the principal.

Suretyship in Hudud and Qisas: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on personal suretyship in hudud and qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding compelling kafalah bil nafs in hudud and qisas, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence of the difference).

Core Idea
Kafalah bil nafs is not compelled in hudud and qisas, but the Sahibayn except qadhf due to servant rights.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about compelling personal suretyship (kafalah bil nafs) in cases of fixed punishments (hudud) and retaliation (qisas). Imam Abu Hanifa held that suretyship cannot be compelled because hudud should be averted; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagreed regarding hadd al-qadhf due to its inclusion of individual rights. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi balancing of divine and servant rights.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's ruling
Identify the core position: one is not compelled to provide personal suretyship in any hadd or qisas case. Note that voluntary kafalah is valid.
The ruling applies to all hudud and qisas.
2
Examine the evidence
The author cites the hadith 'La kafalah fi hadd' and the principle that hudud and qisas should be averted. Understand that the negation is interpreted as absence of compulsion, not prohibition of voluntary giving.
The hadith is from Sunan Bayhaqi, narrated by 'Umar ibn Shu'ayb.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's exception
The Sahibayn hold that in hadd al-qadhf (false accusation of adultery), kafalah can be compelled because it involves rights of the servant (the accused's honor). Distinguish this from other hudud that are purely rights of Allah.
This exception is specific to qadhf.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the accused in qadhf cannot be forced to provide a surety; under the Sahibayn, he can be forced. This affects pre-trial procedures.
The passage does not specify which view is mufta bihi.
5
Note the lack of tarjih
The author simply states the difference without preferring one view. This indicates the ikhtilaf is legitimate within the school.

Source Text

[۱۴۲۷] (۱۰) ولا تجوز الكفالة بالنفس فى الحدود والقصاص عند ابي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى
حدود اور قصاص میں کفالہ بالنفس دینے پر مجبور نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ البتہ وہ دے دے تو کوئی حرج کی بات نہیں ہے۔
اس لئے کہ حدود اور قصاص میں کوشش کی جاتی ہے کہ مجرم پر یہ جاری نہ ہوں۔ اور کفالہ دینے کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ چھوٹتا بھی ہو تو اور مضبوط کیا جائے ۔ اس لئے حد دو اور قصاص میں کفالہ بالنفس دینے پر مجبور نہ کیا جائے (۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ حدثني عمر بن شعیب عن ابيه عن جده ان النبي الا الله قال لا كفالة في حد (ب) ( سنن بیھقی ، باب ماجاء فی الكفالة ببدن من علیہ حق ، ج سادس، ص ۱۲۷، نمبر ۱۱۴۱۷) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ حد میں کفالہ بالنفس نہیں ہے۔ نانه صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ حد قذف میں چونکہ بندے کے حقوق ہوتے ہیں اس لئے اس میں کفالہ بالنفس دینے پر مجبور کیا جائے گا۔
[1427] (10) And suretyship of the person for hudud and qisas is not permissible according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him.
One will not be compelled to give suretyship of the person in hudud and qisas. However, if he gives it, there is no harm.
Because in hudud and qisas, efforts are made that they should not be executed upon the criminal. And the meaning of giving suretyship is to strengthen even if he is released. Therefore, one is not compelled to give suretyship of the person in hudud and qisas. (2) There is a hadith: 'Umar ibn Shu'ayb narrated from his father from his grandfather that the Prophet (PBUH) said: There is no suretyship in a hadd. (Sunan Bayhaqi, Bab ma ja'a fi al-kafalah bi badan man 'alayhi haqq, vol. 6, p. 127, no. 11417) This hadith states that there is no suretyship in hadd. But the Sahibayn say that in hadd al-qadhf, because it involves the rights of servants, one will be compelled to give suretyship of the person.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of compelling kafalah bil nafs (personal suretyship) in cases of hudud (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, one cannot be compelled to provide a surety for the person of the accused in hudud and qisas. However, if someone voluntarily offers such suretyship, it is acceptable. The reason is that the Islamic legal system strives to avert hudud and qisas when possible, and suretyship would strengthen the case against the accused. The proof is a hadith: 'There is no kafalah in a hadd.' The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differ specifically regarding hadd al-qadhf (false accusation of adultery) because it involves the rights of individuals (the accused's honor), so they argue that compelling suretyship is permissible there. The dispute hinges on whether the hadd is purely a right of Allah (haqq Allah) or includes rights of servants. Abu Hanifa views all hudud as primarily rights of Allah; Sahibayn see qadhf as involving servant's rights as well.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): Fixed punishments prescribed by Allah for specific crimes (e.g., theft, zina, qadhf).
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation, e.g., life for life, limb for limb.
كفالة بالنفس (kafalah bil nafs): Personal suretyship: guaranteeing that a person will appear or fulfill an obligation.
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of adultery or fornication; a hadd crime.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in the passage, what is the ruling on kafalah in a hadd?
A) It is required.
B) It is prohibited (haram).
C) There is no kafalah (is not established).
D) It is permissible only with the accused's consent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, kafalah bil nafs is generally not compelled in hudud and qisas, but an exception for qadhf exists according to the Sahibayn, illustrating the tension between divine and servant rights.

Kafalah — Surety

Suretyship of Debt with Unknown Amount
Ruling with proof from Quran and Sunnah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on suretyship for an unknown debt and cite the proofs from Quran and Sunnah that support it.

Core Idea
Suretyship of a debt is valid even if the amount is unknown, due to recurring need and prophetic precedent.

In Hanafi fiqh, suretyship (kafalah) for a debt is a contract of guarantee. A common question arises: is the validity of this contract affected if the exact amount of the debt is not known at the time of the contract? The text establishes that such a contract is fully valid. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its examples, the underlying reasoning (necessity), and the textual proofs from the Quran and hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
The text states that suretyship for debt is valid whether the amount is known or unknown, provided the underlying debt is shar'i (valid).
The condition 'dayn sahih' is essential; if the debt itself is invalid, the suretyship is void.
2
Differentiate between known and unknown amount examples
Known: 'I am guarantor for 1000 dirhams.' Unknown: 'I am responsible for whatever you have against him' or 'whatever comes from this sale.'
In both cases, the suretyship is valid, unlike a sale where the subject matter must be known.
3
Understand the rationale of repeated need
The text says 'mal mein kafeel banne ki baar baar zaroorat parti hai' (there is repeated need to become surety). This necessity (darurah) justifies the relaxation of the knowledge requirement.
This is a Hanafi application of the principle 'al-hajah tanzilu manzilat al-darurah' (need takes the place of necessity).
4
Examine the Quranic proof
The passage cites Surah Yusuf (12:72) where the servant of Prophet Yusuf becomes surety for a camel-load without specifying its quantity. This shows that the Salaf practiced suretyship with unknown amounts.
The camel-load is used as an example of an unspecified guarantee.
5
Examine the prophetic proof
The hadith from Abu Hurairah (Bukhari, Muslim) records the Prophet (PBUH) saying he is responsible for the debts of deceased believers who left no wealth. The amounts of those debts were unknown to the Prophet at the time of his declaration.
The Prophet's practice is the strongest proof for the permissibility of suretyship with unknown debt.

In any case, suretyship of debt is valid, whether the guaranteed thing is known or unknown, provided the debt is valid. For example, he says: I am guarantor on his behalf for a thousand dirhams, or for whatever you have against him, or for whatever will come to you from this sale. ... From here begin the issues of suretyship of debt. So he says: In suretyship of debt, if the property is unknown, i.e., its amount is not known, even then its suretyship is valid. The case of known property is when he says: I am guarantor for a thousand dirhams. And the case of unknown is when he says: I am responsible for whatever comes to you from him. Now, how much will come is not known, yet its suretyship is valid. Or if he says: For whatever comes to you in this sale, I am the surety; now how much will come, its amount is not known, yet the suretyship is valid. Because there is repeated need to become surety for property, so due to necessity, suretyship for the unknown has been declared permissible. (2) In the Quranic verse, the servant of Prophet Yusuf (AS) became surety for a camel-load, and the amount of the camel-load was not known; yet becoming a surety was correct. The verse is: 'And for whoever brings it, a camel-load, and I am its guarantor.' (Surah Yusuf, verse 72) (3) In the hadith, becoming surety for an unknown debt is established. From Abu Hurairah that the Messenger of Allah (PBUH) would be brought a deceased man with debt... 'Whoever among the believers dies and leaves a debt, its repayment is upon me, and whoever leaves wealth, it is for his heirs.' (Bukhari, Kitab al-Kafalah, p. 305, no. 2298; Muslim, Bab man taraka malan fakuru hammat, p. 35, no. 1619) In this hadith, he says: I am the surety for the debt of the believers. Now, how much that debt will come is unknown, yet the Prophet becoming surety was valid. Therefore, becoming surety for unknown property is valid.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that kafalah bil mal (suretyship for a debt) is valid even if the amount of the debt is unknown at the time of the contract, provided the underlying debt itself is valid (shar'i). The text gives examples: known (explicit amount like 1000 dirhams) and unknown (general expressions like 'whatever is due to you from him' or 'whatever comes from this sale'). The validation is based on practical necessity (need) and supported by two proofs: first, from the Quran (Surah Yusuf), where the servant of Prophet Yusuf became surety for a camel-load, the quantity of which was unspecified; second, from the hadith where the Prophet (PBUH) took responsibility for the debts of deceased believers without specifying amounts. The commentary also briefly contrasts kafalah with sale (bay'): sale requires knowledge of the subject matter, but kafalah is valid even with ignorance due to recurring need. This is a distinctive feature of Hanafi law.

Key Terms
مكفول به (makful bihi): The thing guaranteed (the debt or property).
دين صحيح (dayn sahih): A valid debt in Islamic law, resulting from a legitimate transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because sale is more important than suretyship.
B) Because suretyship often involves recurring need, so necessity permits ignorance.
C) Because the Quran specifically allows ignorance in suretyship.
D) Because the Prophet was a surety for unknown debts.

Key Takeaway
Suretyship for a debt is valid in Hanafi law even when the exact amount is unknown, founded on the Quranic precedent of Prophet Yusuf and the Prophet Muhammad's practice, and justified by necessity.

Creditor's Option to Claim from Original Debtor or Surety
Ruling on creditor's option in suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the creditor's option in kafalah and identify the dalil from the hadith of Abu Qatadah.

Core Idea
The creditor may claim from either the original debtor or the surety at his choice.

This lesson clarifies a key feature of kafalah (suretyship) in Hanafi law: the creditor is not restricted to first pursuing the original debtor. Instead, he has a free option to demand payment from either the asil or the kafil. This ruling is supported by a specific hadith that illustrates the direct liability of the surety.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states that the creditor (makful lahu) has the option (khiyar) to demand payment from either the original debtor or the surety.
This establishes joint liability.
2
Understand the nature of kafalah
Kafalah means the surety assumes responsibility for the debt. Both parties become liable.
3
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Abu Qatadah who became surety for a deceased person's debt.
The hadith is the primary proof.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's statement 'Now the skin of the deceased has cooled' indicates that the debt was cleared from the deceased, showing the surety's payment was effective and the creditor could claim from the surety directly.
This is the legal link.
5
Conclude
Thus, the Hanafi ruling is that the creditor has an unrestricted option to claim from either party.

[1429] (12) And the one for whom the guarantee is made (the creditor) has the option: if he wishes, he may demand from the one upon whom the original right is, and if he wishes, he may demand from the surety.
The meaning of suretyship is that the responsibility for the debt falls upon both the original debtor and the surety. Therefore, the creditor may demand the debt from either of them. (2) There is proof in the hadith that Abu Qatadah took responsibility to pay two dinars on behalf of the deceased, and he did not pay until one day passed, which saddened the Prophet; when he paid the next day, the Prophet said: Now the skin of the deceased has cooled. Commentary
This passage states the ruling that the creditor (makful lahu) has the right to demand payment from either the original debtor (asil) or the surety (kafil). The nature of suretyship is that both are liable for the debt. The proof is the hadith of Abu Qatadah, who voluntarily became surety for a deceased person's debt. The Prophet was concerned when payment was delayed, and when Abu Qatadah paid, the Prophet said the deceased's skin had cooled, indicating the removal of the burden. This shows the creditor can directly claim from the surety. The text is cut off, but the intended point is clear: the hadith illustrates the creditor's option.

Key Terms
مكفول له (makful lahu): The creditor, the one for whom the guarantee is made.
أصيل (asil): The original debtor, the one upon whom the original right is.
كفيل (kafil): The surety, the one who guarantees the debt.

Permissibility of Kafalah Bil-Mal for Known or Unknown Debt
Ruling with proof: establishes permissibility of kafalah for unknown debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kafalah for an unknown debt and identify the proofs from Quran, hadith, and the principle of necessity.

Core Idea
Kafalah for an unknown debt is valid, unlike sale, due to necessity and prophetic practice.

Many students assume that a contract of surety, like a sale, requires the exact amount to be known. This lesson clarifies that in the Hanafi school, kafalah bil-mal is valid even when the debt amount is unspecified, provided the underlying debt is valid. We will explore the textual proofs from the Quran and Sunnah that establish this ruling, and understand the difference between kafalah and sale in terms of the required knowledge of the subject matter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage begins by stating the ruling: kafalah for wealth is permissible whether the amount is known or unknown, as long as it is a valid debt. Identify the act (kafalah bil-mal) and its hukm (jaiz/permissible).
The matn is from Al-Quduri, a foundational Hanafi text.
2
Understand the scope of permissibility
The commentary gives examples: known amount (e.g., 'I guarantee a thousand dirhams') and unknown amount (e.g., 'whatever you are owed' or 'whatever arises from this sale'). Note that the unknown amount is not a barrier to validity.
The unknown amount could be a variable debt from a transaction.
3
Analyze the Quranic proof
The author cites Surah Yusuf 12:72, where the servant of Prophet Yusuf guaranteed a camel-load — an unknown quantity. The word 'za'im' means guarantor. Explain how this verse proves the permissibility of kafalah for an unknown amount.
The verse is evidence that the amount need not be known.
4
Analyze the hadith proof
The hadith of Abu Hurairah is quoted: the Prophet ﷺ guaranteed the debts of believers who died in debt. The debt amount could be unknown, yet the guarantee was valid. This is a direct sunnah proof.
The hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim, indicating strong authenticity.
5
Distinguish kafalah from sale
The passage explicitly contrasts kafalah with sale: sale requires the subject matter to be known, while kafalah does not. This distinction is crucial. Reflect on the underlying rationale: necessity (hajah) in financial transactions allows leniency.
The distinction is based on the different nature of the two contracts.

كتاب الكفالة
حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى [۲۸ ) واما الكفالة بالمال فجائزة معلوما كان المكفول به او مجهولا اذا كان دينا صحيحا مثل ان يقول تكفلت عنه بالف درهم او بما لک علیه او بما يدركك في هذا البيع 
بہر حال کفالہ بالمال تو جائز ہے، مکفول یہ معلوم ہو یا مجہول ہو جبکہ دین صحیح ہو مثلا یہ کہے کہ اس کی جانب سے ضامن ہوں ہزار درہم کا یا جو کچھ تیر اس کے ذمہ ہے یا جو کچھ آپ کو اس بیع میں لگے گا ۔۔ ر یہاں سے کفالہ بالمال کے مسئلے شروع ہیں۔ پس فرماتے ہیں کہ کفالہ بالمال میں مال مجہول ہو یعنی اس کی مقدار معلوم نہ ہو تب بھی اس کا کفالہ جائز ہے۔ مال معلوم ہو اس کی صورت یہ ہے کہ میں ہزار درہم کا ضامن ہوں۔ اور مجہول کی صورت یہ ہے کہ کہے آپ کے ذمہ جو کچھ آتا ہے میں اس کا ذمہ دار ہوں۔ اب کتنا آئے گا یہ معلوم نہیں ہے پھر بھی اس کا کفالہ جائز ہے۔ یا یوں کہے کہ اس بیع میں آپ کے ذمے جو کچھ آئے گا میں اس کا کفیل ہوں، اب کتنا آئے گا اس کی مقدار بھی معلوم نہیں ہے اس کے باوجود کفالہ صحیح ہے۔ مال میں کفیل بننے کی بار بار ضرورت پڑتی ہے اس لئے ضرورت کی بنا پر مجہول کفالت کو بھی جائز قرار دے دیا گیا (۲) آیت میں حضرت یوسف علیہ السلام کے خادم ایک اونٹ بوجھ کے کفیل بنے تھے اور اونٹ کے بوجھ کی مقدار معلوم نہیں ہے اس کے با وجود کفیل بنا درست تھا۔ آیت ہے۔ ولـمـن جـاء بـه حـمـل بعير وانا به زعیم (آیت ۷۲ سوره یوسف (۱۲) (۳) حدیث میں مجہول دین کا کفیل بننا ثابت ہے۔ عن ابی ھریرۃ ان رسول الله الله كان يوتى بالرجل المتوفى عليه الدين ... فمن توفى من المؤمنين فترك دينا فعلى قضاؤه ومن ترک مالا فلورثته (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب الدین، کتاب الکفالۃ ص ۳۰۵ نمبر ۲۲۹۸ ر مسلم شریف باب من ترک مالا فکور همت ص ۳۵ نمبر ۱۶۱۹) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ میں مومنین کے دین کا کفیل ہوں، اب وہ دین کتنا آئے گا یہ مجہول ہے اس کے باوجود حضور کیا کفیل بنا صحیح ہوا۔ اس لئے مجبول مال کا کفیل بنا صحیح ہے۔
وت کفالہ اور بیج میں فرق ہے کہ بیع معلوم شی ء کی ہی ہو سکتی ہے اور کفالہ مجہول چیز کا بھی ہوسکتا ہے۔
ما یدرک : یہ درک سے مشتق ہے، جو آپ پر آئے ، جو کچھ آپ کو پالے۔
Book of Kafalah. Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him: [28] As for kafalah for wealth, it is permissible whether the guaranteed item is known or unknown, provided it is a valid debt, such as one saying, 'I guarantee for him a thousand dirhams,' or 'whatever you have against him,' or 'whatever occurs to you in this sale.'
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on Kafalah bil-Mal (suretyship for a debt). The matn states the ruling: kafalah for a debt is permissible regardless of whether the amount is known or unknown, as long as it is a valid debt. The commentary explains that the ruling covers both known amounts (e.g., 'I guarantee a thousand dirhams') and unknown amounts (e.g., 'I guarantee whatever you are owed' or 'whatever arises from this sale'). The justification is necessity (hajah), as people frequently need suretyship in transactions. The proof is from the Quran (Surah Yusuf 12:72) where the servant of Prophet Yusuf guaranteed a camel-load, which was an unknown quantity. Also from the hadith of Abu Hurairah where the Prophet ﷺ guaranteed the debts of the believers who died in debt, and the debt amount could be unknown. The commentary distinguishes kafalah from sale (bay'): sale requires the subject matter to be known, while kafalah is valid even with unknown amounts due to its nature as a gratuitous guarantee.

Key Terms
كفالة بالمال (kafalah bil-mal): A contract of suretyship for financial obligation, where the kafil (surety) guarantees the debt of another.
مكفول به (makful bihi): The subject matter of kafalah, i.e., the debt or obligation being guaranteed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is kafalah bil-mal valid if the amount of debt is unknown?
A) No, it is invalid because the subject must be known.
B) Yes, it is valid due to necessity and evidence from Quran and Sunnah.
C) Only if the creditor agrees.
D) Only if the debtor agrees.

Key Takeaway
Kafalah for an unknown debt is permissible in the Hanafi school, supported by the Quran, hadith, and the principle of necessity, and it is distinguished from sale which requires knowledge of the subject matter.

Suspending Kafalah Upon a Condition Is Valid
Permits making kafalah conditional on a future event, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional kafalah, identify the dalil, and apply it to practical examples.

Core Idea
Kafalah can be suspended on a future condition; the Prophet ﷺ guaranteed debts conditionally.

In many financial dealings, a person may need to guarantee a future or unknown obligation. Is such a conditional guarantee valid? The Hanafi school answers with a clear yes, based on prophetic practice and the need for flexibility in transactions. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being discussed? (Ta'liq al-kafalah - suspending guarantee on a condition). What is the ruling? (Jaiz - permissible).
2
Examine the examples given
The text provides three examples: 'Whatever so-and-so sells, it is on me,' 'Whatever becomes due on him from you, it is on me,' 'Whatever so-and-so usurps from you, it is on me.' Notice that the amount is unknown at the time of contract.
These examples show that even unknown future obligations can be guaranteed.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The proof is a hadith where the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Whoever leaves a debt, it is on me.' This is a conditional kafalah (if the believer dies with debt). The hadith also mentions that the guarantor (Abu Qatada) paid the debt and the deceased's skin cooled.
The Prophet's statement is a conditional guarantee, supporting the permissibility.
4
Understand the rationale (hikmah)
The commentary explains that such flexibility is needed daily. Without ta'liq, many common guarantees (e.g., for future sales, loans) would be invalid.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools require the debt to be known at the time of kafalah. The Hanafi school holds that ta'liq is valid because the need for it is widespread.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text

[۱۴۳۰](۱۳) ويجوز تعليق الكفالة بالشروط مثل ادا کرنے سے پہلے جس طرح کفیل پر ذمہ داری ہے اسی طرح اصیل پر بھی ذمہ داری ہے کہ وہ عذاب میں مبتلا ر ہے۔ حدیث یہ ہے ۔ قــال
جابر توفى رجل فغسلناه وحنطناه وكفناه ثم اتينا النبي الا الله فقلنا له تصلى عليه فقام فخطا خطى ثم قال عليه دين؟ قال فقيل ديناران قال فانصرف قال فتحملهما ابو قتادة قال فاتيناه قال فقال ابو قتادة الديناران على فقال النبي الا الله حق الـغـريـم وبــرى منهما الميت قال نعم فصلی علیه رسول الله الا الله قال فقال له بعد ذلك بيوم ما فعل الديناران؟ قال انما مات امس قال فعاد اليه كالغد قال قد قضيتهما فقال النبى الله الله الآن بردت عليه جلده (الف) (سنن سیحقی ، باب الضمان عن لیمیت، ج سادس، ص ۱۲۴، نمبر ۱۱۴۰۵) اس حدیث میں اس وقت تک میت کی چھڑی ٹھنڈی نہیں ہوئی جب تک کہ دونوں دینار ابو قتادہ نے ادا نہ کر دئیے ۔ جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ دو دینار کی ذمہ داری اصل میت پر بھی رہی ۔ اس لئے کفیل اور مکفول عنہ
دونوں ذمہ دار ہوں گے۔
اور کفالت میں کفیل اور اصیل دونوں ذمہ دار ہوتے ہیں۔
(۱۴۳۰] (۱۳) اور جائز ہے کفالہ کو معلق کرنا شرط کے ساتھ مثلا یہ کہے کہ جتنا فلاں کے ساتھ بیچے وہ مجھ پر ہے یا جو تیرا اس کے ذمہ واجب ہو وہ مجھ پر ہے، تیری جو چیز فلاں غصب کرے وہ مجھ پر ہے۔

کفالہ میں اس قسم کی وسعت ہے۔ اس لئے یہ قابل برداشت ہے۔ کیونکہ روزانہ اس کی ضرورت پڑتی ہے (۲) حدیث میں ہے کہ آپ اس شرط پر دین ادا کرنے کے کفیل بنے کہ اگر مومنین نے قرض چھوڑا تو اس کی ادائیگی مجھ پر ہے۔ عـن جــابـر قـال كان النبي الله لا يـضـلـى على رجل عليه دين ... من ترک دینا فعلی و من ترك مالا فلورثته (( نسائی شریف، الصلوۃ علی من علیه دین
It is permissible to suspend kafalah upon conditions, such as ...
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of suspending (ta'liq) kafalah on a condition. The matn states that kafalah can be conditional, for example: 'Whatever so-and-so sells, it is on me,' or 'Whatever becomes due on him from you, it is on me,' or 'Whatever so-and-so usurps from you, it is on me.' The commentary explains that just as unconditional kafalah is valid, so is conditional kafalah. Examples from the matn illustrate that the kafil guarantees an unknown future obligation. The proof cited is the hadith of the Prophet ﷺ who said: 'Whoever leaves a debt, it is on me,' which is a conditional kafalah (if the believer dies in debt). The necessity (hajah) for such flexibility is mentioned, as people frequently need to guarantee unknown future obligations.

Key Terms
تعليق الكفالة (ta'liq al-kafalah): Making the kafalah contingent upon a future event or condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is conditional kafalah (ta'liq) valid in Hanafi fiqh?
A) No, because the subject must be certain.
B) Yes, it is valid and supported by prophetic practice.
C) Only if the condition is already fulfilled.
D) Only for known debts.

Key Takeaway
Conditional kafalah is valid in the Hanafi school, supported by the Prophet's ﷺ practice and the need for flexibility in transactions.

Conditional Kafalah: Validity and Evidence
Ruling on conditional kafalah with evidence from Hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional kafalah and identify the conditions for its validity based on the hadith of Abu Qatadah.

Core Idea
A kafalah suspended on a known future condition is valid and enforceable.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in guarantees: can you promise to cover a debt or liability that will only arise in the future? The Hanafi school, relying on the practice of the Prophet ﷺ, says yes—provided the condition is specific and known. We will examine the ruling and the proof from the hadith of Jabir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states: 'It is permissible to suspend kafalah on conditions.' This is the hukm: jaiz (permissible). Note that it is a voluntary act, not obligatory.
The ruling is permissibility, not obligation.
2
Understand the examples of valid conditions
The text gives three examples: guaranteeing a future sale price, a future due debt, or future usurped property. These all involve specific, identifiable future events.
The condition must be known (ma'lum), not speculative.
3
Analyze the dalil from Hadith
The hadith of Abu Qatadah shows the Prophet accepted a conditional guarantee: Abu Qatadah said, 'The two dinars are on me,' and only then did the Prophet pray over the deceased. This proves the validity of suspending kafalah on a condition.
The hadith is from Sunan Bayhaqi and is ahad (zanni al-thubut).
4
Derive the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's acceptance of the conditional guarantee and subsequent action (prayer) indicate that such a kafalah is valid and effective. The condition (Abu Qatadah's assumption) was known and accepted.
The proof lies in the Prophet's explicit approval.
5
Note the practical scope
The ruling applies to any kafalah with a known future condition. If the condition is uncertain (e.g., 'if it rains'), the kafalah is invalid due to gharar.
This protects both guarantor and creditor from excessive uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز تعليق الكفالة بالشروط مثل ان يقول ما بايعت فلانا فعلی او ما ذاب لک علیه فعلی او ما غصبک فلان فعلی. عن جابر قال توفى رجل فغسلناه وحنطناه وكفناه ثم اتينا النبي الله فقلنا له تصلى عليه فقام فخطا خطى ثم قال عليه دين؟ قال فقيل ديناران قال فانصرف قال فتحملهما ابو قتادة قال فاتيناه قال فقال ابو قتادة الديناران على فقال النبي الله حق الغريم وبري منهما الميت قال نعم فصلی علیه رسول الله قال فقال له بعد ذلك بيوم ما فعل الديناران؟ قال انما مات امس قال فعاد اليه كالغد قال قد قضيتهما فقال النبي الله الآن بردت عليه جلده.
It is permissible to suspend kafalah on conditions, such as he says: 'What I sell to so-and-so is on me' or 'What is due to you from him is on me' or 'What so-and-so usurps from you is on me'. From Jabir: A man died, we washed him, embalmed him, and shrouded him, then we came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and said, 'Pray over him.' He stood and took a few steps, then said, 'Does he owe a debt?' It was said, 'Two dinars.' He turned back. Abu Qatadah assumed responsibility for them. We came to him and Abu Qatadah said, 'The two dinars are on me.' The Prophet said, 'Is the creditor's right fulfilled and the deceased free from them?' He said, 'Yes.' Then the Messenger of Allah prayed over him. The next day he asked, 'What happened to the two dinars?' Abu Qatadah said, 'He only died yesterday.' He asked again the following day, and he said, 'I have paid them.' The Prophet said, 'Now his skin has cooled.'
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil). The matn states that kafalah (guarantee) can be suspended upon conditions, such as promising to guarantee a future sale price, a debt that may become due, or property that may be usurped. The Hanafi school considers this valid because it is a form of guarantee that is subject to everyday needs and is supported by the practice of the Prophet. The hadith of Abu Qatadah demonstrates that the Prophet allowed a conditional guarantee: Abu Qatadah guaranteed the deceased's debt on the condition that the creditor accepted it. The Prophet then prayed over the deceased only after the debt was secured. This shows that a conditional kafalah is enforceable and benefits the guarantor and debtor alike. The commentary adds that the condition must be known (not speculative), so saying 'if it rains I will guarantee' is invalid due to uncertainty. The Hanafi position: suspending kafalah on a known condition is valid.

Key Terms
تعليق الكفالة بالشروط (ta'liq al-kafalah bi al-shurut): Suspending a guarantee contract upon a future condition that triggers liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the correct Hanafi ruling on a kafalah suspended on the condition 'if you buy a car from him'?
A) It is invalid because the condition is future.
B) It is valid because the condition is known and specific.
C) It is invalid unless the seller agrees.
D) It is valid only if the price is already fixed.

Key Takeaway
A kafalah can be validly conditioned on a specific, known future event, as supported by the hadith of Abu Qatadah.

Guarantor's Liability Upon Proof of Debt
Ruling on guarantor's liability after proof of debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a guarantor's liability when a general guarantee is followed by proof of the exact debt amount.

Core Idea
A general guarantee becomes binding once the debt amount is proved by evidence.

In the contract of kafalah (suretyship), a guarantor may make a general statement like 'I guarantee whatever is due from him.' This leaves the debt amount unspecified. This lesson clarifies that such a guarantee is not void for uncertainty; rather, once the creditor presents legal proof (bayyinah) of a specific debt, the guarantor becomes obligated to pay that exact amount. The ruling ensures that a general guarantee remains enforceable and does not simply lapse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
The act is kafalah (guarantee), a contract of surety. In this case, the guarantee is general in wording — the debtor did not name a specific amount.
General guarantees are valid in the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the role of proof (bayyinah)
The creditor brings bayyinah (legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females) that the debtor owes a specific amount (here, one thousand). This proof establishes the exact debt.
Bayyinah is the mechanism that transforms the general guarantee into a specific obligation.
3
Derive the ruling
Upon proof, the guarantor becomes liable (dhaman) for the proven amount. The general wording is interpreted as an absolute undertaking; proof crystallizes the liability.
The guarantor cannot argue that the amount was not specified originally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا قال تكفلت بما لك عليه فقامت البينة بالف عليه ضمنــه الكفيل
And if he says 'I guarantee what is due from him' and proof is established that one thousand is due from him, then the guarantor becomes liable.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This is a clear ruling statement (matn) regarding the liability of a guarantor when a debt is proven through evidence. The guarantee was general ('what is due from him'), so once bayyinah (proof) establishes the exact amount, the guarantor must pay accordingly. The commentary explains that the general statement is treated as an absolute guarantee; proof makes it specific. The guarantor cannot deny liability once evidence is presented.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Legal evidence, usually testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a guarantor says 'I guarantee what is due from him' and the creditor brings two witnesses that the debtor owes 500 dirhams, what is the ruling?
A) The guarantor is not liable because the amount was not specified initially.
B) The guarantor is liable for 500 dirhams.
C) The guarantor is only liable if the debtor admits.
D) The guarantor must pay half of 500 dirhams.

Key Takeaway
A general guarantee becomes binding once the debt amount is proven by legal evidence; the guarantor cannot escape liability based on initial vagueness.

Guarantor's Statement with Oath in Absence of Proof
Ruling on accepting guarantor's statement with oath when no proof exists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedural ruling when a guarantor is sued for a debt and the creditor has no evidence, and explain the burden of proof based on the hadith.

Core Idea
In absence of proof, the guarantor's sworn statement is accepted: burden is on the claimant.

In a kafalah (guarantee) contract, disputes may arise about the amount owed. The general rule in Islamic evidence law is that the claimant must bring proof. This lesson presents the Hanafi ruling when the creditor fails to establish evidence: the guarantor, as the defendant, takes an oath and his statement is accepted regarding the amount he admits. This ensures fairness and prevents unjust demands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and roles
In this dispute, the creditor (mukful lah) is the claimant (mudda'i). The guarantor (kafil) is the defendant (mudda'a 'alayh).
2
Determine the evidence requirement
According to the hadith 'al-bayyinatu 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yaminu 'ala al-mudda'a 'alayh' (the burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the defendant), the claimant must bring evidence (bayyinah).
This hadith is cited by the commentator from Tirmidhi and Bukhari.
3
Apply the condition of no evidence
If the claimant fails to establish proof, the defendant's position is accepted. The guarantor makes an oath (yamin) confirming his claim about the debt amount.
The oath is taken after the claimant's request; the form is 'Wallahi' or similar.
4
State the ruling
Thus, the ruling is: the guarantor's statement with his oath is accepted regarding the amount he admits. He is only liable for what he confesses under oath.
This is a procedural ruling (hukm shar'i), not a hukm taklifi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان لم تقم البينة فالقول قول الكفيل مع يمينه في مقدار ما يعترف به
And if proof is not established, then the statement of the guarantor (is accepted) with his oath, regarding the amount he admits.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This is a ruling statement (matn) on the burden of proof in guarantee cases. The creditor (mukful lah) is the claimant (mudda'i) and must bring evidence. If he fails, the guarantor (kafil) is considered the defendant (mudda'a 'alayh). According to the hadith 'al-bayyinatu 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yaminu 'ala al-mudda'a 'alayh', the guarantor's statement under oath is accepted. He must swear that the debt is no more than what he admits. The commentary explicitly cites this hadith from Tirmidhi and Bukhari to support the ruling.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath sworn by the guarantor to confirm his claim about the debt amount.
بينة (bayyinah): Proof, evidence; typically two witnesses or a written document that is legally acceptable.
كفيل (kafil): Guarantor; one who is responsible for the debt of another.
مدعي (mudda'i): Claimant; the party who brings a claim and must provide evidence.
مدعى عليه (mudda'a 'alayh): Defendant; the party against whom the claim is made, who may take an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a kafalah dispute, if the creditor has no witnesses, what happens?
A) The guarantor must pay whatever the creditor claims.
B) The guarantor's statement with oath is accepted.
C) The case is dismissed.
D) The debtor's statement is accepted.

Key Takeaway
When a creditor lacks proof, the guarantor's sworn statement determines his liability—a direct application of the burden of proof rule.

Principal Debtor's Admission Does Not Bind Guarantor
Ruling that the principal debtor's admission does not bind the guarantor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a principal debtor's admission does not bind the guarantor and explain the reasoning based on separate liability.

Core Idea
The guarantor's liability is based on his own admission, not the debtor's.

In guarantee contracts, the principal debtor might later admit to owing more than the guarantor acknowledged. This lesson clarifies that such an admission does not increase the guarantor's liability. Understanding this rule protects the guarantor from being held liable for debts he did not commit to.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
The lesson involves two parties: the principal debtor (makful 'anhu) and the guarantor (kafil). The guarantor has already made an admission or been proven liable for a certain amount.
2
Understand the admission scenario
The principal debtor admits to a debt larger than what the guarantor admitted. This admission is made outside the presence of the guarantor or without corroboration.
3
Apply the ruling
State that the principal debtor's admission does not bind the guarantor. The guarantor remains liable only for the amount he himself admitted or that was proven against him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان اعترف المكفول عنه باكثر من ذلك لم يصدق على كفيله
And if the one guaranteed for (the principal debtor) admits to more than that, he is not believed against his guarantor.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This is a final ruling in the sequence. The principal debtor (makful 'anhu) might admit to a larger debt than what the guarantor admitted under oath. However, his admission does not bind the guarantor because the guarantor is a separate party. The guarantor's liability is based on his own admission (or evidence against him). The principal debtor's confession only affects himself, not the guarantor. The commentary clarifies that even if the principal debtor admits to a higher amount, the guarantor is only liable for the amount he himself admitted (or was proven).

Key Terms
المكفول عنه (al-makful 'anhu): The principal debtor for whom the guarantee is made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 1000 dirhams because the debtor admitted.
B) 500 dirhams because the guarantor's admission is binding on him.
C) Nothing because of contradiction.
D) The guarantor must pay 750 dirhams.

Key Takeaway
The principal debtor's admission is not binding on the guarantor; the guarantor's liability is based solely on his own commitment.

Kafalah is Valid with or without Permission
States the permissibility of suretyship with or without the principal's consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of kafalah with or without the principal's permission and cite the textual evidence supporting it.

Core Idea
Kafalah is valid even without the principal's permission — the guarantor's own wealth is his to spend.

Many assume that a suretyship (kafalah) is only valid if the principal (the person being guaranteed) gives permission. The Hanafi school, however, holds that permission is not a condition for validity. This lesson examines the proofs from hadith and the underlying principle that a person has full disposal over his own wealth, making kafalah permissible in both cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the opening statement: 'Kafalah is valid with the command of the principal and without his command.' This is the core ruling.
The Arabic phrase 'وتجوز الكفالة بامر المكفول عنه وبغير امره' clearly states permissibility in both scenarios.
2
Locate the First Dalil
The passage cites the hadith 'al-za'imu gharim' (the guarantor is liable). This general statement implies that liability is assumed regardless of the principal's consent.
This hadith is general ('amm') in Hanafi usul, covering all cases of kafalah.
3
Analyze the Second Dalil
The story of Abu Qatada from Sahih al-Bukhari shows that the Prophet accepted a guarantee for a deceased person's debt without the deceased's permission. This is a specific application of the ruling.
This incident is a 'fi'l' (action) of the Prophet and a companion, providing direct precedent.
4
Understand the Underlying Principle
The text notes that the guarantor has his own wealth and can dispose of it freely. Since guaranteeing a debt is a disposition of wealth, permission is not needed from the principal.
The principle 'al-insan yatasarrafu fi malihi' (one can dispose of one's own property) is a foundational usul maxim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكفالة [1434] (17) وتجوز الكفالة بامر المكفول عنه وبغير امره. کفالہ جائز ہے مکفول عنہ کے حکم سے اور بغیر اس کے حکم سے۔ کفیل دونوں طرح بنتا ہے۔ مکفول عنہ کے حکم سے بنے تب بھی بنتا ہے اور بغیر اس کے حکم کے اپنی مرضی سے کفیل بنے تب بھی کفیل بن سکتا ہے۔ پیچھے حدیث گزری الزعيم غارم (ابوداؤد شریف نمبر ۳۵۶۵) اس حدیث میں دونوں طرح سے کفیل بننے کا امکان موجود ہیں۔ اس لئے حکم اور بغیر حکم دونوں طرح کفیل بن سکتے ہیں (۲) کفیل کا اپنا مال ہے اس لئے بغیر مکفول عنہ کے حکم بھی خرچ کر سکتا ہے (۳) حضرت ابو قتادہ بغیر میت کے حکم کے قرض کے کفیل بنے تھے۔ عن سلمة بن اکوع ان النبي الا الله الى بجنازة ليصلى عليها ... قال ابو قتادة على دينه يا رسول الله فصلی علیه (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب من تكفل عن میت دینا فلیس له ان مرجع ص ۳۰۶ نمبر ۲۲۹۵) اس حدیث میں حضرت ابو قتادہ بغیر حکم کے کفیل بنے ہیں۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ اپنے مال میں جائز تصرف کر سکتا ہے۔
Book of Kafalah. [1434] (17) And kafalah is valid with the command of the principal and without his command. Kafalah is permissible with the permission of the principal and without his permission. The guarantor becomes a guarantor in both ways: if he becomes with the principal's permission, he becomes a guarantor, and if without his permission, by his own choice, he can become a guarantor. Earlier the hadith passed: 'The guarantor is responsible' (Abu Dawood no. 3565). In this hadith, there is possibility of becoming guarantor in both ways. Therefore, one can become guarantor with or without permission. (2) The guarantor has his own wealth, so he can spend it without the principal's permission. (3) Abu Qatada became guarantor for a debt without the permission of the deceased. From Salamah ibn al-Akwa' that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was brought a funeral to pray over... he said: Abu Qatada said: 'His debt is upon me, O Messenger of Allah,' so he prayed over him. (Bukhari, chapter on whoever becomes guarantor for a deceased's debt, he has no recourse...) In this hadith, Abu Qatada became guarantor without permission. Principle: This issue is based on the principle that one can freely dispose of one's own wealth.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states the fundamental permissibility of kafalah (suretyship) in Hanafi law: it is valid whether the principal (makful anhu) gives permission or not. The author provides three proofs: (1) The hadith 'al-za'imu gharim' (the guarantor is liable) which implies the guarantor's assumption of liability regardless of the principal's consent. (2) The guarantor's own wealth is his to spend; he may use it to discharge a debt without needing permission. (3) The incident of Abu Qatada who guaranteed the debt of a deceased person without the deceased's permission, and the Prophet accepted it. The underlying usul principle is that a person can dispose of his own property as he wishes (sahih al-tasarruf fi malihi).

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of suretyship where one person (the guarantor) assumes liability for a debt or obligation of another (the principal).
مكفول عنه (makful anhu): The principal or debtor for whom the guarantee is made.
غارم (gharim): One who is liable or responsible; the guarantor is described as gharim (liable) in the hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is kafalah valid if the principal (makful anhu) does not give permission?
A) No, it is invalid without permission
B) Yes, it is valid because the guarantor can dispose of his own wealth
C) It is valid only if the guarantor is a relative
D) It is valid but the guarantor cannot pay without permission

Key Takeaway
Kafalah is valid in Hanafi law regardless of the principal's consent, based on the guarantor's right over his own wealth and the practice of the Prophet.
