And if he becomes surety for the buyer regarding the price, it is permissible. (25) And if he becomes surety for the seller regarding the sale, it is not valid. (26) And whoever hires an animal for carrying, if it is a specific animal, the kafalah for the carriage is not valid. (27)... (25) He can pay the same amount that the buyer has, but rather he can pay its like from his own side. Therefore, becoming a surety is valid. (2) There is an athar: 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar did not see any harm in pledging collateral and a surety along with a loan (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab jawaz al-rahn wa al-hamil fi al-salaf, vol. 6, p. 19). In this athar, 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar did not consider it harmful to become a surety in a sale. (3) In Bukhari, there is a long hadith about a man from Bani Isra'il who asked someone for a loan of one thousand dinars. The seller demanded a surety for the price, and he said, 'Allah is enough as a surety.' The part of the hadith is: Abu Hurayra reported that the Messenger of Allah (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) mentioned a man from Bani Isra'il who asked someone to lend him a thousand dinars. He said, 'Bring me witnesses that I may witness them.' He said, 'Allah is sufficient as a witness.' He said, 'Then bring me a surety.' He said, 'Allah is sufficient as a surety.' He said, 'You have spoken the truth.' (Bukhari, Bab al-kafalah fi al-qard wa al-duyun wa al-abdan wa ghayriha, p. 305, no. 2291). From this hadith, it is understood that becoming a surety for the price is permissible; that is why this bounty was asked for a surety and he said, 'Allah is sufficient as a surety.' This issue is based on the principle that wherever one can give the like from his own side, he can become a surety. And wherever one cannot give the like from his own side, but rather it is necessary to give the very same thing that the principal debtor has, then he cannot become a surety. (26) If he becomes a surety for the seller regarding the sale (i.e., delivery of the sold item), it is not valid. If the surety says, 'I will force the seller to deliver the item to you,' then the surety is valid. But if he becomes surety by saying, 'If he does not hand over the item, I will give the item from my side,' then such a surety is not valid. In the sale, it is necessary to give the exact item that was agreed upon; giving its like from one's own side is not valid. Therefore, one cannot become a surety. It will be such that if the seller does not hand over the item, the sale will be rescinded and the seller will receive nothing of the price. This issue is based on the principle that one cannot become a surety for something whose like he cannot give from his own side. (27) Someone hired a riding animal for carriage; if it is specific (ayn), the kafalah for the carriage is not valid. A man hired a riding animal for carriage; if the animal is specific, such that the carriage is to be on that animal, then becoming a surety for it is not valid.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents three scenarios illustrating the principle that kafalah is valid when the surety can provide the like (mithl) of the obligation from his own side. (25) Kafalah for the price (thaman) is valid because the price is a debt; the surety can pay its equivalent. The text supports this with an athar from Ibn 'Umar and a hadith from Bukhari about the man from Bani Isra'il. (26) Kafalah for the sold item (mabi') is invalid if the surety promises to deliver the same item from his own side, because the sale contract requires the exact item to be delivered. However, if the surety merely guarantees to compel the seller to deliver, that is valid (as a guarantee of performance). (27) In a hire contract for a specific animal, kafalah for the carriage is invalid because the carriage is specified to that animal; the surety cannot provide the same carriage from his own side. The underlying principle is that where the obligation is a specific thing (ayn) that cannot be substituted, kafalah is invalid; where it is a debt (dayn) that can be paid by the like, kafalah is valid.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): Price paid in a sale, a financial debt that can be paid by equivalent.
مبيع (mabi'): The object of sale; a specific item that must be delivered exactly as contracted.
مثل (mithl): A like or equivalent, used to determine if a surety can fulfill the obligation from his own resources.
عين ('ayn): A specific, identifiable object; its delivery is required exactly, not a substitute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is kafalah for the price in a sale valid, but kafalah for the sold item invalid?
A) Because the price is more valuable than the item.
B) Because the price is a debt (dayn) that can be paid by the like, while the sold item is a specific 'ayn that must be delivered exactly.
C) Because the buyer has already paid the price, so the surety only needs to guarantee the seller.
D) Because the price is always cash, while the item may be perishable.

Key Takeaway
The validity of kafalah hinges on whether the surety can provide the mithl of the obligation; debts are guaranteeable, specific things are not.

Guarantee for the Price by the Buyer is Valid
Ruling with proof from hadith and athar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on guarantee for a price in a sale, explain the proof from the hadith of Bani Israel and the athar of Abdullah ibn Umar, and apply the condition that the guarantor must be able to give an equivalent.

Core Idea
A guarantee for a debt is valid because the guarantor can give the equivalent from himself.

This passage establishes a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh: it is permissible for a person to become a guarantor (kafil) for the price (thaman) owed by a buyer. The author provides two proofs: the practice of Abdullah ibn Umar and a prophetic hadith about a man from Bani Israel. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the underlying principle that guarantee is valid when the obligation can be fulfilled by giving an equivalent (mithl) rather than a specific identical item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the exact ruling: a guarantee for the price in a sale is valid (جائز). Note that it is not obligatory – it is a permissible contract.
The key Arabic phrase is 'تكفل عن المشترى بالثمن جاز'.
2
Locate the first proof (athar)
Find the athar from Abdullah ibn Umar: he saw no harm in a pledge and a guarantor along with a loan. This shows the permissibility from the practice of a Companion.
The athar is from Sunan al-Bayhaqi.
3
Locate the second proof (hadith)
Find the hadith of the man from Bani Israel who asked for a loan and offered Allah as a guarantor. The Prophet (peace be upon him) did not object, indicating approval of such a guarantee.
The hadith is in Bukhari (no. 2291).
4
Extract the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's approval of the man's statement 'Allah is sufficient as a guarantor' indicates that it is permissible to have a guarantor for a debt. The hadith shows the practice of guaranty in a loan/sale context.
The Prophet did not rebuke the request for a guarantor, so it is allowed.
5
Understand the underlying principle
The author states that the guarantee is valid because the guarantor can give the equivalent (mithl) of the price from himself. If the obligation required the exact same specific item (like a specific camel), guarantee would not be valid because one cannot force another to hand over that specific item.
This distinguishes between mithli (fungible) and qimi (non-fungible) obligations.

He guarantees for the buyer regarding the price, it is valid. [1443] (26) He gives the very same money that the buyer has, rather he can give its equivalent from himself. Therefore becoming a guarantor is correct. (2) There is an athar that there is no harm in becoming a guarantor for the price. Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states that if a person becomes a guarantor (kafil) for the buyer regarding the price (thaman) of a sale, it is valid. The reason is that the guarantor can pay the same amount or its equivalent from his own property; he is not required to give the exact physical money that the buyer has. The text then provides two proofs: (1) An athar from Abdullah ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he saw no harm in a pledge and a guarantor along with a loan. (2) A hadith from Bukhari about a man from Bani Israel who was asked for a guarantor and said 'Allah is sufficient as a guarantor,' and the Prophet (peace be upon him) affirmed it. This shows that asking for a guarantor for a debt is permissible. The underlying principle is that a guarantee is valid when the guarantor can discharge the obligation by giving the equivalent (mithl) of what is owed; if the obligation requires the very same specific item (like a specific animal), then guarantee is not valid because one cannot force another to hand over a specific item.

Key Terms
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of guarantee where a person (kafil) becomes responsible for a debt or obligation of another (makful 'anhu) in favor of a creditor (makful lahu).
ثمن (thaman): The price in a sale transaction; a debt owed by the buyer to the seller.
مثل (mithl): An equivalent or similar item, typically used for fungible goods (mithliyyat) like money, grains, etc.

Key Takeaway
A guarantee for a debt is valid because the guarantor can give an equivalent (mithl) from his own property.

Kafalah — Surety
intermediate

Guarantee for the Sold Item by the Seller is Invalid
Ruling explaining why guarantee for a specific sold item is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on guaranteeing delivery of a specific sold item and explain the underlying principle that a guarantor cannot substitute a unique asset.

Core Idea
A guarantee to deliver a specific sold item is invalid because the guarantor cannot provide an equivalent (mithl) for a unique asset.

In this passage, we examine a specific type of guarantee (kafalah) that arises in sales: a third party guaranteeing that the seller will deliver the sold item. The Hanafi school distinguishes between two scenarios: one where the guarantor merely promises to pressure the seller, and another where he promises to deliver the item himself. The ruling turns on the nature of the sold item—whether it is a fungible or a specific, non-fungible asset. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying kafalah contracts in commercial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a guarantee (kafalah) made by a third party for the seller regarding the delivery of the sold item (mabi'). The ruling stated is that such a guarantee is invalid (batil) under certain conditions.
The passage uses 'لم تصح' indicating invalidity.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
The passage contrasts two forms of the guarantee. First: if the guarantor says, 'I will pressure the seller to hand over the item,' the guarantee is valid. Second: if the guarantor says, 'If the seller does not deliver, I will give the item from myself,' the guarantee is invalid.
The key is what the guarantor undertakes to do.
3
Understand the reasoning for invalidity
The sold item (mabi') is a specific, non-fungible object (ayn). The contract requires delivery of that exact item. The guarantor cannot substitute an equivalent because the buyer is entitled to the very thing agreed upon.
Compare with fungibles (mithliyat) where an equivalent is acceptable.
4
Extract the underlying principle
The passage states the principle: a guarantee is not valid for something for which the guarantor cannot give an equivalent (mithl) from himself. This applies to unique items (a'yan) but not to debts or fungibles.
This principle governs kafalah for delivery of specific goods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان تكفل عن البائع بالبيع لم تصح [ ۱۴۴۴ (۲۷) اگر بائع کی جانب سے مبیع کا کفیل بنے تو صحیح نہیں ہے۔ اگر یوں کفیل نے کہ میں بائع پر زور دوں گا کہ وہ بیچ آپ کے حوالے ضرور کرے تب تو کفیل بنا صحیح ہے۔ لیکن یوں کھیل بنے کہ وہ صبیح نہیں دے گا تو میں اپنی جانب سے مبیع دے دوں گا تو ایسا کفیل بنا صحیح نہیں ہے۔ مبیع میں ضروری ہے کہ وہی چیز دے جو طے ہوئی ہے۔ اس کی مثل دوسری چیز اپنی جانب سے دوں گا یہ مطابقت نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے کفیل بھی نہیں بن سکتا۔ اتنا ہوگا کہ بائع مبیع حوالے نہیں کرے گا تو بیع ختم ہو جائے گی اور بائع کو قیمت میں کچھ بھی نہیں ملے گی۔ انوں یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ اپنی جانب سے جس چیز کی مثل نہیں دے سکتا ہو اس کا کفیل بنا صحیح نہیں ہے۔
And if he guarantees for the seller regarding the sale, it is not valid. [1444] (27) If the guarantor says: I will pressure the seller to hand over the sold item to you, then the guarantee is valid. But if the guarantee is such that he says: if he does not give the sold item, I will give the sold item from myself, then such a guarantee is not valid. In the sold item, it is necessary to give the very thing that was agreed upon. To give a different equivalent from oneself is not conformity. Therefore he cannot become a guarantor. It will be such that if the seller does not hand over the sold item, the sale will be terminated and the seller will get nothing of the price. They said this issue is based on the principle that one cannot become a guarantor for something for which one cannot give the equivalent from oneself.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement. It addresses the situation where a person becomes a guarantor for the seller regarding the delivery of the sold item (mabi'). The ruling is that such a guarantee is invalid if the guarantor promises to deliver the sold item himself. The reason is that the sold item is often a specific, non-fungible object (e.g., a particular house or animal). The guarantor cannot give an equivalent because the buyer is entitled to the exact item specified in the contract. However, if the guarantor merely promises to force the seller to deliver, then it is valid because he is not undertaking to deliver the item himself. The underlying principle is that a guarantee is only valid for debts or obligations that can be discharged by an equivalent (mithl); for specific items (a'yan), the guarantor cannot provide a substitute.

Key Terms
مبيع (mabi'): The sold item, often a specific, non-fungible asset (ayn) in a sale contract.
كفيل (kafil): The guarantor who assumes responsibility for an obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a guarantee for the sold item (mabi') invalid if the guarantor says 'I will give the sold item from myself'?
A) Because the guarantor cannot force the seller.
B) Because the sold item is a specific object that cannot be substituted with an equivalent.
C) Because the buyer did not accept the guarantee.
D) Because the price is unknown.

Key Takeaway
A guarantee for delivery of a specific sold item is invalid if the guarantor promises to deliver the item himself, because the buyer is entitled to the exact item contracted.

Guarantee for Loading: Specified vs Unspecified Animal
Ruling on guarantee when animal is unspecified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a valid guarantee of loading (haml) on a hired animal and distinguish it from the case where the animal is specified.

Core Idea
Guarantee is valid only when the animal is unspecified, as an equivalent can be given.

This passage from the matn addresses a specific scenario in kafalah (guarantee) combined with ijara (hire). The key distinction is whether the hired animal is specified or not. Understanding this helps in determining the validity of guarantees for services involving fungible objects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Identify the act: a guarantee (kafalah) for loading (haml) on a hired animal. The ruling: if the animal is unspecified, the guarantee is valid (jā'iz).
2
Locate the Condition
Find the condition that triggers validity: the animal must be unspecified (ghayr 'ayn). The text says 'if it is without specification, the guarantee is valid'.
3
Understand the Rationale
The rationale is that the obligation is for a fungible (mithli) item—the guarantor can give an equivalent animal. The text states: 'if one can give its equivalent from oneself, then becoming a guarantor is correct'.
This contrasts with specified (mu'ayyan) items where exact delivery is required.
4
Contrast with Specified Animal
If the animal were specified (e.g., a particular black horse), the guarantee would be invalid because the guarantor cannot ensure the exact same animal is provided.
This is the negative implication (mafhūm al-mukhālafah) of the text.
5
Note the Underlying Principle
The general principle: guarantee is valid when the subject matter is mithli (fungible) because the guarantor can fulfill by giving an equivalent.
This principle applies in other kafalah scenarios as well.

Kafala of an Unspecified Riding Animal
Ruling on validity of kafala when the animal is unspecified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that determines the validity of kafala for a riding animal in Hanafi fiqh and apply the principle of misl.

Core Idea
Kafala of an unspecified animal is valid; kafala of a specified animal is invalid because the kafil cannot provide a similar item.

This lesson examines a ruling from Hanafi fiqh regarding kafala (suretyship) when the subject is a riding animal. The key distinction is whether the animal is specified or unspecified. The principle of misl (similar item) determines validity: if the kafil can provide a similar animal, the contract is valid; if not, it is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is kafala (suretyship) for a riding animal. The passage states two rulings: valid if unspecified, invalid if specified.
2
Identify the condition
The condition that flips the ruling is 'specification' (tayin). If the animal is specified (mu'ayyan), the kafala is invalid; if unspecified (ghayr mu'ayyan), it is valid.
The specification can be explicit or implicit in the wording.
3
State the underlying principle
The principle is that the kafil must be able to fulfill the obligation by providing a similar item (misl). If the animal is specified, the kafil cannot produce an identical one, so the contract is invalid. If unspecified, he can provide any similar animal.
This applies to fungible goods (misl) in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Apply to a new scenario
Imagine a kafil says, 'If Zaid does not return the borrowed horse, I will give my own horse.' The horse is specified, so kafala is invalid. If he says, 'I will provide a horse for loading,' unspecified, it is valid.

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of kafala (suretyship) when the subject is a riding beast (sawari) to be used for loading (haml). The commentator explains the principle: kafala requires that the kafil be able to fulfill the obligation from his own resources if the original debtor defaults. Condition of Creditor's Acceptance in Kafala: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling and dispute on creditor acceptance condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on whether creditor acceptance is required for kafala and apply the mufta bihi to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Kafala can be valid without creditor acceptance; the fatwa follows the more lenient view.

This lesson examines a key condition in the contract of kafala: the creditor's acceptance. While the primary text states acceptance in the session is necessary, the Hanafi school also preserves an alternative view that dispenses with this requirement. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for correct fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling as stated in the matn
The matn states that kafala is not valid except with acceptance of the creditor (makful lahu) in the contract session. The immediate ruling is that acceptance is a condition for validity.
This is the surface-level ruling, but the text immediately introduces a dispute.
2
Understand the rationale for requiring acceptance in the session
The creditor may not want to deal with a new person (the kafil) if he is wicked. Acceptance ensures consent. The session requirement is because offer and acceptance must meet; otherwise the offer lapses.
This rationale is given in the commentary (sharh).
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Abu Hanifa has another narration that acceptance is not necessary at all. Imam Abu Yusuf holds that acceptance can be outside the session. Their reasoning: the kafil benefits the creditor, so consent is presumed. Analogy to fuduli marriage.
The two positions within the Hanafi school are clearly stated.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The text explicitly states that the fatwa is on the view that acceptance is not necessary. Thus, for practice, kafala is valid even without the creditor's acceptance.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۴۴۶] (۲۹) نہیں صحیح ہے کفالہ مگر مکفول لہ کے قبول کرنے سے مجلس عقد میں ۔ جس مجلس میں کفیل بن رہا ہو اس مجلس میں مکفول لہ نے قبول کیا ہو کہ ہاں میں فلاں کے کفیل بننے سے راضی ہوں تب کفالت صحیح ہوگی ۔ تو گویا کہ دو شرطیں ہوئیں۔ ایک مکفول لہ کا قبول کرنا اور دوسری شرط یہ ہے کہ مجلس کفالت میں قبول کرے اس سے باہر قبول کرے تو کفالت صحیح نہیں ہوگی۔ آدمی آدمی میں فرق ہوتا ہے۔ کوئی شریف ہوتا ہے اور کوئی شریر ہوتا ہے۔ اب تک مکفول لہ کا واسطہ براہ راست مقروض سے تھا۔ کفالت کے بعد اس کا واسطہ کفیل سے بھی ہوگا اور ممکن ہے کہ وہ شریر ہو جس کی بنیاد پر وہ کھیل سے واسطہ نہ رکھنا چاہتا ہو۔ اس لئے کفالت کی بنیاد پر کفیل

بن جائے گا۔
کفیل اپنی جانب سے رقم دینے کے لئے کہ رہا ہے اور مکفول لہ کو فائدہ ہے کہ پہلے ایک سے مطالبہ کر سکتا تھا اب دو سے مطالبہ کرے گا۔ اور دونوں میں سے کسی ایک سے وصول کرے گا۔ اس لئے مکفول لہ کے قبول کرنے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے (۲) یہ توثق اور اعتماد کے لئے

الشرح الثميرى الجزء الثاني
۳۱۳
كتاب الكفالة
الا بقبول المكفول له فى مجلس العقد ۱۴۴۷ (۳۰) الا في مسئلة واحدة و هي ان يقول المريض لوارثه تكفل عنى بما على من الدين فتكفل به مع غيبة الغرماء جاز ۱۳۸ (۳۱) واذا كان الدين على اثنين وكل واحد منهما كفيل ضامن عن الآخر فما بھی کفیل بن جائے گا۔ جیسے کہ فضولی کے نکاح کو مجلس کے علاوہ جہاں خبر ملے اور قبول کر لے تب بھی نکاح ہو جاتا ہے۔ اسی طرح مجلس کفالت کے علاوہ میں ممکفول لہ قبول کر لے تب بھی کفالت درست ہو جائے گی۔
(29) Kafala is not valid except with acceptance of the makful lahu in the session of the contract. In whichever session the kafil is being made, the makful lahu should have accepted in that session saying 'Yes, I am pleased with so-and-so being a kafil.' Then kafala will be valid. So there are two conditions: one is acceptance by the makful lahu, and the second condition is that he accepts in the session of kafala; if he accepts outside it, kafala will not be valid. People differ: some are noble and some are wicked. Until now, the makful lahu had direct dealing with the debtor. After kafala, he will also deal with the kafil, and it is possible that the kafil is wicked, on account of which he may not want to deal with him. Therefore, for establishing a relationship with the kafil on the basis of kafala, his consent and acceptance are needed. So acceptance by the makful lahu is necessary. The principle: this issue is based on the principle that the makful lahu's claim and relationship will be with a new person, so his consent is necessary. And the need for acceptance in the session is because the kafil gives an offer (ijab), so he should accept in the session; otherwise, his offer becomes void. Because the contract of kafala is concluded by the offer of the kafil and the acceptance of the makful lahu. Imam Abu Hanifa's other narration is that acceptance of the makful lahu is not necessary for being a kafil; he becomes a kafil even without acceptance. The kafil is proposing to give money from himself, and the makful lahu benefits: previously he could demand from one, now he will demand from two, and will collect from either. Therefore, acceptance by the makful lahu is not necessary. (2) This is for strengthening and confidence: that because of kafala, his wealth will not be lost. Therefore, acceptance by the makful lahu is not necessary. The fatwa is on this. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, it is not necessary for the makful lahu to accept in the session of kafala; rather, wherever he hears of it and accepts, then [it is valid]. Just as in the case of a fuduli (unauthorized agent) marriage, if he hears and accepts outside the session, the marriage occurs. Similarly, if the makful lahu accepts outside the session of kafala, then also kafala will be valid.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage deals with the condition of acceptance (qabul) by the creditor (makful lahu) for the validity of kafala. The text initially states that kafala is not valid except with acceptance in the very session of the contract. The commentator explains the rationale: the creditor may not want to deal with a new person (the kafil) if he is wicked, so his consent is needed. Acceptance must be in the session because offer and acceptance are essential for a contract; if acceptance comes later, the offer is deemed withdrawn. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: Imam Abu Hanifa's other narration (riwaya) holds that acceptance is not necessary because the kafil is voluntarily obligating himself and the creditor benefits. Similarly, Imam Abu Yusuf holds that acceptance can be outside the session, analogizing to a fuduli marriage. The relied-upon position (fatwa) is that acceptance is not necessary for validity (i.e., kafala is valid even without acceptance). This shows the flexibility within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
مکفول لہ (makful lahu): The creditor or beneficiary of the kafala; the person to whom the debt is owed.
ایجاب (ijab): The offer made by the kafil to guarantee the debt.
قبول (qabul): Acceptance by the creditor of the kafala offer.
فضولی (fuduli): An unauthorized agent who acts on behalf of another without permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, it is required in the same session.
B) Yes, but it can be outside the session.
C) No, acceptance is not required.
D) It depends on the type of debt.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, kafala is valid without the creditor's acceptance, as per the relied-upon fatwa.

Exception in Kafala: Heir Guaranteeing Debt of Terminally Ill
Ruling on exception to acceptance requirement in kafala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi exception to the acceptance requirement in kafala and explain why it is reclassified as a wasiyyah.

Core Idea
A sick person's heir can guarantee debts without creditor acceptance because it is really a will, not a guarantee.

In this lesson, we examine a unique exception in Hanafi fiqh where the normal requirement of acceptance in kafala is waived. The case involves a terminally ill person appointing an heir as a guarantor for debts, even when creditors are absent. The commentator reveals that this is not true kafala but a testamentary disposition, thus bypassing the usual conditions. Let's explore the rationale and implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recall that in kafala (suretyship), the acceptance of the creditor (makful lahu) is normally a condition for the contract to be valid.
This condition ensures the creditor's agreement to the substitution of debtor.
2
State the exception
When a person in terminal illness (marad al-mawt) asks his heir to become kafil for his debts, the kafala is valid even without the creditors being present or accepting.
The passage explicitly states this as the only exception.
3
Understand the reclassification
The commentator explains that this is not true kafala; in reality, it is a wasiyyah (will) to pay the creditors. The heir acts as an executor (wasi), not as a kafil.
This reclassification is key to understanding why the normal rule is waived.
4
Analyze the rationale
The rationale is practical: at the time of death, all creditors may not be present, and without such appointment, the debts could go unpaid. Thus, the contract is valid by necessity and by its nature as a will.
The Hanafi school accommodates exceptional circumstances to protect creditors' rights.
5
Note the legal effect
The heir becomes obligated to pay the debts from the estate as an executor, not as a personal guarantor. The validity does not depend on creditor acceptance.
If the sick person recovers, the arrangement may revert to normal kafala rules? (Not discussed in passage.)

Source Text

(30) Except in one case, and that is: if a sick person says to his heir, 'Become a kafil on my behalf for the debt that is on me,' and he becomes a kafil for it in the absence of the creditors, it is valid. This is one case where even if the makful lahu does not accept, rather the makful lahu is absent, still kafala is valid. That is: a person is in terminal illness (marad al-mawt) and tells his heir, 'Become a kafil for the debt on me,' and he becomes a kafil in the absence of the creditors. In that case, the heir's being a kafil is valid. This is not really kafala; in reality, it is a will (wasiyyah) to pay the creditors. And the kafil is in reality an executor (wasi). Therefore, in this case, becoming a kafil without acceptance by the makful lahu is valid. (2) There is also a constraint because at the time of death, all creditors are not present. If a kafil or executor is not appointed, the creditors' debt would be lost. Therefore, becoming a kafil without acceptance by the makful lahu is valid. Ut al-ghurama': creditors, lenders.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage presents an exception to the general rule that kafala requires creditor acceptance. The exception is when a person in terminal illness (marad al-mawt) asks his heir to become a kafil for his debts. Even if the creditors are absent and have not accepted, the kafala is valid. The commentator explains that this is not true kafala; it is essentially a testamentary appointment (wasiyyah) where the heir acts as an executor (wasi) to ensure the debts are paid. The rationale is practical: at death, creditors may not be present, and without such appointment, the debts could go unpaid. Thus, the contract is valid by necessity and by its nature as a will. This shows how the Hanafi school accommodates exceptional circumstances.

Key Terms
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): Terminal illness from which one is expected to die.
وصیت (wasiyyah): A will or testamentary disposition.
وصی (wasi): Executor of a will; one appointed to fulfill the testator's wishes.

The Hanafi exception for a sick person's debt kafala by an heir is valid because it is effectively a will, thus requiring no creditor acceptance.

Recovery in Mutual Kafala: Only Excess Over Own Share
Ruling on recovery in mutual kafala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on recovery between mutual sureties by calculating the recoverable amount as the excess over one's own share.

Core Idea
Payment first satisfies your own debt; only amounts beyond your share can be recovered from a co-surety.

When two persons are jointly liable for a debt and each is a surety (kafil) for the other, a payment made by one must first be applied to his own share. Only the surplus over his own half can be reclaimed from his partner. This lesson works through the underlying principle: the principal debt (asl) takes priority over the subsidiary guarantee (far').

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two persons are co-debtors (e.g., each owes 500) and mutual sureties. One pays a certain amount.
2
Determine the payer's own share
Calculate half of the total debt as the payer's own liability. In a 50/50 split, his share is 50%.
3
Compare the payment to his share
If payment ≤ his share, it is all applied to his own debt. If payment > his share, the surplus is treated as payment on behalf of the co-debtor via kafala.
4
Apply the rule of recovery
The payer can recover only the excess over his own share from the co-debtor. He cannot recover any part of his share payment.
Reason: the asl (own debt) is satisfied first; the far' (kafala) kicks in only after that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۴۴۸] (۳۱) اگر قرض دو آدمیوں پر ہو اور دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کفیل اور ضامن ہو دوسرے کا تو جو کچھ ان میں سے ایک نے ادا کیا تو وہ شریک سے وصول نہیں کرے گا یہاں تک کہ زیادہ ہو جو ادا کیا آدھے سے، پس وصول کرے گا زیادہ کو۔
دو آدمیوں پر قرض تھا۔ مثلا دو آدمیوں نے ایک غلام ایک ہزار میں خریدا تھا اور دونوں پر آدھی آدھی قیمت قرض تھی یعنی پانچ پانچ سو ور ہم تھے ۔ اور دونوں ایک دوسرے کے کفیل بھی تھے۔ پس ایک نے اگر آدھا قرض یعنی پانچ سو ادا کیا ہے تو یہ آدھا خود اس کے حصے کا شمار کیا جائے گا، شریک کے حصے کا شمار نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ اس لئے جب تک آدھا ادا کیا تو اس میں سے کچھ شریک سے وصول نہیں کرے گا۔ ہاں آدھا سے زیادہ ادا کرے تو اپنے شریک سے وصول کرے گا۔

(31) If the debt is on two persons, and each of them is a kafil and guarantor (damin) for the other, then whatever one of them pays, he will not recover from his partner until what he paid is more than half; then he will recover the excess. Two persons had a debt. For example, two persons purchased a slave for one thousand, and on both was half the price as debt, i.e., five hundred each. And both were kafils for each other. So if one paid half the debt, i.e., five hundred, this half will be counted as his own share, not counted as the partner's share. Therefore, as long as he paid half, he will not recover anything from the partner. Yes, if he pays more than half, he will recover from his partner. Half the debt is the principal (asl) and is his own liability. And kafala is subsidiary (far') and demand. The rule is that the principal comes first and the subsidiary later. Therefore, what he paid up to half is paid from his side as principal debt, not as kafala on behalf of the partner. So he cannot recover anything from the partner. However, whatever he pays beyond half, that
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage deals with the case of joint debtors who are also mutual sureties (each is kafil for the other). The ruling: if one pays an amount equal to or less than his own share (half of the total debt), he cannot recover anything from the co-debtor. Only the excess over his share can be recovered. The commentator illustrates with an example: two persons jointly buy a slave for 1000, each owing 500, and each guarantees the other. If one pays 500, that covers his own debt; he cannot claim from the other. If he pays 700, he can recover 200 from the co-debtor. The principle is that the obligation to pay one's own debt (asl) takes precedence over the subsidiary kafala obligation. Payment is applied first to the payer's own debt; only after that is it considered payment on behalf of the co-debtor via kafala.

Key Terms
اصل (asl): Principal; the primary debt obligation of the debtor himself.
فرع (far'): Subsidiary; the surety obligation (kafala) which is secondary to the principal.
كفيل (kafil): Surety; one who guarantees the debt of another.
ضامن (damin): Guarantor; synonym for kafil in this context.

Key Takeaway
A mutual surety can only recover the amount paid in excess of his own share from the co-debtor.

Mutual Suretyship: Recourse Between Co-Sureties
Ruling on mutual suretyship recovery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on recourse between co-sureties in mutual kafalah and apply it to a numerical example.

Core Idea
When two sureties mutually guarantee each other, one paying recovers half from the other.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school on kafalah. When two individuals act as sureties for a debtor and also guarantee each other, the payment by one creates a right to recover half from the co-surety. We will explore the rationale and the options available to the paying surety.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
State the scenario: two sureties guarantee a debt with mutual suretyship (each is a surety for the other). The ruling: if one surety pays any amount, he recovers half from the co-surety.
2
Understand the rationale
Analyze why half is recoverable: since each surety is also a guarantor for the other, half of the payment is deemed paid on behalf of the co-surety.
Both sureties are equal in their secondary status (furu').
3
Note the alternatives
The paying surety may choose to recover half from the co-surety or the full amount from the original debtor (aseel), since the debt was originally his.
4
State the principle
When two sureties are equal in subordination, payment is apportioned equally. Each pays half in their own capacity and half as surety for the other.
5
Apply to an example
If the debt is 1000 dirhams and one pays 600, he recovers 300 from the co-surety. Then both together pursue the original debtor.
This illustrates the proportional right of recourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اذا تكفل اثنان عن رجل بالف على ان كل واحد منهما كفيل عن صاحبه فما اداه احدهما يرجع بنصفه على شريكه قليلا كان او كثيرا دو آدمی ایک آدمی کے ایک ہزار درہم کے کفیل بنے۔ پھر یہ دونوں کفیل آپس میں بھی ایک دوسرے کے کفیل بن گئے تو مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک کفیل جتنا دا کرے گا اس کا آدھا اپنے شریک کفیل سے وصول کرے گا مثلا پانچ سو ادا کیا ہو تو ڈھائی سو اپنے شریک کفیل سے لے گا۔ پھر دونوں ملکر اصیل سے وصول کریں گے۔ یہاں دونوں کفیلوں پر ذاتی قرض نہیں ہے بلکہ دونوں پر کفالت ہے اور فرع ہے اس لئے دونوں فرع ہونے میں برابر ہے۔ اور چونکہ دونوں ایک دوسرے کے کفیل اور ضامن ہیں اس لئے جو کچھ ادا کیا اس کا آدھا اپنی جانب سے ادا کیا اور آدھا بطور کفالت کے شریک کی جانب سے ادا کیا۔ اس لئے آدھا اس سے وصول کر سکتا ہے۔ اور یہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ شریک کے بجائے اصل مقروض سے وصول کرے۔ کیونکہ اصل میں تو اسی کا قرض ادا کیا ہے۔ صول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ دونوں برابر درجے کے فروع ہوں تو آدھا شریک سے وصول کر سکتا ہے۔ اس لئے کہ جو کچھ ادا کیا اس میں سے آدھا اپنے شریک کی جانب سے بطور کفالت ادا کیا۔
If two men become sureties for a man for a thousand, provided that each one of them is surety for his companion, then what one of them pays, he recovers half from his partner, be it little or much. Two people became sureties for one man for one thousand dirhams. Then these two sureties also became sureties for each other. The ruling is: whatever one surety pays, he will recover half from his fellow surety. For example, if he paid five hundred, he will take two hundred and fifty from his fellow surety. Then both together will recover from the principal. Here, neither surety has a personal debt; rather both have suretyship, and both are secondary parties. So both are equal in being secondary. And since both are each other's sureties and guarantors, whatever was paid, half was paid from himself and half was paid as suretyship on behalf of his partner. Therefore, he can recover half from him. And it is also possible that he recovers from the original debtor instead of the partner, because in reality he paid his debt. The principle: when two are equal in subordination, one can recover half from the partner, because half of what was paid was paid as suretyship on behalf of his partner.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement (hukm) from the Hanafi school on mutual suretyship. It addresses a scenario where two individuals act as sureties (kafeel) for a single debtor (aseel) for a sum of 1000 dirhams, with the additional condition that each surety also guarantees the other (i.e., mutual kafalah). The ruling: when one surety pays any amount to the creditor, he may recover exactly half of that paid amount from his co-surety, regardless of whether the amount is small or large. The underlying reason: since each surety is also a guarantor for the other, the paying surety is considered to have paid half on his own behalf and half as a representative (wakil) of the co-surety. Therefore, he has the right to reclaim that half from the co-surety. Alternatively, he may choose to recover the entire amount from the original debtor (aseel), because the debt was originally the aseel's. The principle established: when two sureties are equal in their status as secondary obligors (furu'), each payment is apportioned equally between them. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that a surety's liability is secondary and can be shared proportionally in cases of mutual guarantee.

Key Terms
كفيل (kafeel): A person who assumes liability for another's debt or obligation; a surety.
كفالة (kafalah): A contract of suretyship where one person guarantees the debt or presence of another.
شريك (shareek): Partner or co-surety in this context; the other surety who is mutually guaranteed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 600 dirhams
B) 300 dirhams
C) 200 dirhams
D) 0 dirhams

Key Takeaway
In mutual suretyship, a paying surety recovers exactly half from his co-surety, reflecting the proportional liability of equal secondary obligors.

Invalidity of Suretyship for a Mukatib's Debt
Ruling with hadith proof that kafalah for a mukatib's debt is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why suretyship (kafalah) for a mukatib's debt is invalid in the Hanafi school, and identify the key reason as the contingent nature of the mukatib's obligation.

Core Idea
Suretyship for a mukatib's debt is invalid because the debt is not absolutely binding – it falls if the mukatib becomes unable to pay.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from the Hanafi school concerning kafalah (suretyship/guarantee). The case involves a mukatib – a slave who has contracted with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments. The question is whether a third party can become a surety for that debt. The Hanafi position is clear: no, it is invalid. The reason lies in the unique nature of the mukatib's obligation, which is conditional and not permanently binding. We will also consider a supporting hadith that underscores the seriousness of debt obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is kafalah (suretyship) for the debt of a mukatib (the mal al-kitabah). The ruling is that it is not permissible (ghair ja'iz), meaning the contract of kafalah is void (batil).
The ruling applies regardless of whether the surety is free or a slave.
2
Understand the principal's (mukatib's) debt
The mukatib has taken a debt from his master under the kitabah contract. This debt is not a standard absolute debt; if the mukatib becomes unable to pay, he reverts to slavery and the debt is cancelled. Hence, the obligation is contingent.
Contrast with a normal debt where the debtor remains liable even if he becomes insolvent.
3
Link the contingent nature to invalidity of kafalah
In Hanafi fiqh, valid kafalah requires that the principal's obligation be absolutely binding (lazim). Since the mukatib's debt is conditional and may fall away, the suretyship is invalid because there is no fixed, enforceable debt to guarantee.
This is the core legal reasoning behind the ruling.
4
Consider the hadith proof
The text includes a hadith: a martyr, even if resurrected multiple times, will not enter Paradise until his debt is paid. This hadith emphasizes the gravity of debt but does not directly prove the invalidity of kafalah for a mukatib. It is cited to stress that debts must be taken seriously, yet the specific ruling is derived from the nature of the mukatib's obligation.
The hadith serves as a general reinforcement, not a direct proof for the mukatib case.
5
Confirm the Hanafi position
The established Hanafi position (zahir al-riwayah) is that such suretyship is invalid. This is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) for fatwa. Some other scholars may differ, but within the Hanafi school, the ruling is settled.
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah from the core Hanafi texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1450] (33) نہیں جائز ہے کفالہ مال کتابت کا چاہے آزاد اس کا کفیل بنے چاہے غلام ۔ مکاتب نے کتابت کے لئے مولی کا قرض اپنے سر لیا۔ اس قرض کا کوئی کفیل بننا چاہے تو کفیل نہیں بن سکتا۔ حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے فرمایا کوئی آدمی اللہ کے راستے میں شہید ہو جائے پھر زندہ کیا جائے پھر شہید ہو جائے پھر زندہ کیا جائے تو اس پر قرض ہو تو اس وقت تک جنت میں داخل نہیں کیا جائے گا جب تک کہ قرض ادا نہ کر دیا جائے ۔
The suretyship for the property of kitabah (writing contract) is not permissible, whether a free person becomes surety for it or a slave. The mukatib took the master's debt upon himself for the kitabah. If anyone wants to become surety for this debt, he cannot become surety. Footnote: He (the Prophet) said: 'If a man is martyred in the path of Allah, then resurrected, then martyred, then resurrected, then martyred, and he has a debt, he will not enter Paradise until his debt is paid.'
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with a supporting hadith proof from the Hanafi school. It declares that kafalah (suretyship) for the mal al-kitabah (the debt owed by a mukatib slave under a contract of manumission) is invalid (ghair ja'iz). This applies whether the surety is a free person or a slave. The reason is explained later in the text: the mukatib's debt is not a binding obligation because if the mukatib becomes unable to pay the installments, he reverts to slavery and the debt falls away. Since the principal (makful 'anhu) is not under a binding obligation, the suretyship is invalid. The hadith cited (about martyrdom not forgiving debt) serves as a general principle emphasizing the seriousness of debt, but the specific ruling is derived from the contingent nature of the mukatib's debt. The Hanafi school thus restricts valid kafalah to debts that are absolutely binding on the principal.

Key Terms
كفالة مال الكتابة (kafalah bi mal al-kitabah): Suretyship for the debt owed by a mukatib (slave under contract of manumission) to his master under the kitabah contract.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah (written agreement) to purchase his freedom by paying installments to his master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is suretyship for a mukatib's debt invalid in the Hanafi school?
A) Because the mukatib is a slave and cannot own property
B) Because the mukatib's debt is contingent and falls if he becomes unable to pay
C) Because the master's consent is required
D) Because the debt is not a financial obligation

Key Takeaway
A valid suretyship requires the principal's obligation to be absolutely binding; the contingent nature of a mukatib's debt makes kafalah for it invalid in the Hanafi school.
